Anyone refusing to obey a chief’s orders or rejecting his decision in a dispute was liable to internment in the “calaboose” spoken of so approvingly by the old man at the beginning of this paper. The might of the German, and, in turn, the Japanese and American administrations stood behind a chief’s words; and although some kind of appeal from his decisions was usually possible, the likelihood of anyone refusing to obey his clearly expressed mandates and escaping unscathed had become rather small.' [377] Prior to colonization, direct coercion and sorcery were employed instead: 'There were no police. A chief's brothers or sons might act on his behalf to intimidate or attack someone who had offended him. But it was control of magical power, either by the chief or one his brothers or sons, that made improper conduct liable to punishment. Major craft specialists could also make ill those who violated the taboos of their craft. Finally, members of chiefly lineages and their close associates were likely to have knowledge of sorcery. All such knowledge gave punitive power to chiefs and important specialists. People stressed maintaining the appearance of propriety in behavior so as not to give just cause for offense.' [378] 'Sorcery is a more direct if more secret form of aggression and is practically precluded from being applied within the lineage by the fact that the spells used are in each case the property of a given lineage. While it is not clear whether they would be considered effective, it is very doubtful that they would be used against others who knew them or could be expected to know them. Data on sorcery are difficult to obtain for no one admits to being a sorcerer. Those cases of afflictions (blindness, psychosis, and paralysis) which were definitely attributed to a given sorcerer always applied to someone on another island, angry because his wife had been taken away, his property trespassed upon, or the like. Although various people on Romonum were said to know certain techniques of sorcery and many people expressed a fear of being sorcerized, no one would say that a given person had actually sorcerized anyone. The detailed body of knowledge of procedures in sorcery which exists and the circumstantial accounts of how it was formerly used certainly suggest very strongly that in the past Trukese deliberately sorcerized other Trukese. Whether this is the case today, however, is an open question. In the past [Page 150] it was used by master magicians, both against rival magicians and against the laity, and was also used in the punishment of thieves; but then as now sorcery was primarily the result of one person’s animosity against another and as such acted as a sanction against acts which might antagonize another. Thus anxiety about sorcery is another factor weighing the balance in favor of conformist behavior.' [379] 'Another weak point of the chiefs is partiality. Members of the chief’s kin group, blood relatives, and friends are not readily punished, not even for serious crimes. On the other hand, poor devils whom the chief cannot bear are sentenced to hard labor for the slightest reason or are reported to the government as “disobedient ones” or “rebels.” In the old days the Truk chiefs did away with troublesome persons by means of the knife; today they do it by means of lies and false denunciations.' [380] It is therefore difficult to ascertain how the resolution of disputes was handled prior to colonization. We have assumed that the resolution (or strong-arming) of disputes by chiefs and their brothers fits in better with 'non-state judiciary' than with 'legal system'.

♠ Religious judiciary ♣ absent ♥ We have found no evidence for mediums and diviners playing a formal judicial role. The following statement seems ambiguous: 'Ritual practices were conducted by their own specialists. They included spirit mediums, breadfruit summoners, fish summoners, healers, masters of spells, masters of sorcery, builders, navigators, diviners, and most importantly the masters of magic and ritual relating to war and politics. Their knowledge was private property passed down to their children and junior lineage mates.' [381] Further confirmation is needed.

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ present ♥ Land was held by both individuals and corporate descent groups: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [382] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [383] Women commonly held property, but husbands and children retained usufruct rights over the property of mothers and wives: 'The right to private property exists among the Truk natives. Everyone of them, be it man or woman, adult or child, can own property, for example, land, houses, cattle, money, etc. Whatever the wife brings into the marriage remains wholly at her disposal.' [384] 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.' [385] Elder brothers held supervision rights over property owned by the sib: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [386] According to Bollig, disputes over land rights were common: 'The land rights of the Truk people are not based on justice, but on the right of the stronger, the cleverer. There is hardly a plot of land with a clear legal history, and for that reason there are the constant assertions of legal claims, the eternal lawsuits over land. The boldest and most cunning persons always have the best land on Truk. Pretenses for bringing legal claims to foreign property before the chief are easily found in the confused conditions. And once they are found, the Kanakas’ inexhaustible power of invention and art of lying take care of making them convincing to others. That briberies of chiefs, interpreters, and witnesses play a tremendous part in this is obvious, considering the character of the Truk people. No means is too wicked for them in order to “profit. ”' [387] 'A source of land disputes is the exchange of lands. For example: Soni and Soledon exchange two pieces of land. Soni now cultivates his land, while Soledon lets it lie fallow. Soledon dies. His children, who are not on good terms with Soni, cancel the exchange and take possession of the productive piece of land as property of their deceased father. Soni resists. The parties quarrel for years. Sometimes the one wins, sometimes the other, but no one yields.' [388] In practice, the boundaries between land owned by different individuals could be vague: 'The boundaries of the lands are very vague. They change continually under the hands of both neighbors. Sometimes the one, sometimes the other one, goes beyond them. Then they quarrel. What A has planted B tears out, with the argument that it is already on his territory. What B has planted A tears out, with the same argument. Now which of the two is right?' [389] 'With such uncertain legal conditions it is not at all surprising that quarrels over land are daily events on Truk. Every death, every divorce, every new marriage has a coil of rats’ tails in quarrels as a result. The boundless egotism of the Truk natives is responsible for all this. Everybody wants to have as much as possible. A Trukese can quarrel for years about a tree, a plant, or a foot of land, even though he has plenty of trees that are not producing anything because of lack of care. The German government gave the natives land documents on which the owner, along with all his pieces of land, was recorded. /123/ The hopes placed on this have not been fulfilled. Today everything is as confused as before.' [390] Rights in land were differentiated from rights in plantings: 'The Truk people are familiar with a distinction of the ground from what is planted on it. Thus, somebody may possess a piece of land, but the trees that stand on it belong to other people. They or their parents once planted the trees on his property, which is permissible according to Truk customs. If the owners of the trees are dishonest, they need only clear the field beginning from the trees, continue to cultivate, and the poor owner is dispossessed. What confused situations that causes in the case of an eventual transfer of the piece of land to another is easily understandable. I myself have had difficulties for years, since certain people still held on to “their” trees after the piece of land had long been the property of the mission. Only the felling of the trees put an end to all claims.' [391] Ownership was claimed and asserted through material markings: 'The natives try to document their right of ownership to pieces of land by clearing something on it from time to time, or they build huts on the individual pieces of land, which they then inhabit temporarily. Dying fathers give their children directions to bury them on their pieces of land so that their grave might thus be proof of ownership for the future. One can often hear in the kobu[unknown] that this is precisely what the people refer to.' [392] 'The riri, that is, to bind, forms a strange title deed on the Truk Islands. For example, somebody sees a tree which bears many fruits. Since he would like some too, he takes the ubud of a coconut palm and wraps it around a branch of the tree in order to make his intention known publicly. In many cases the owner resists such oppression and tears off the ubud. But also in many cases he keeps quiet in order to avoid bigger quarrels.' [393] 'I myself have had experiences with the riri in that the girls “bound” pineapples for themselves on my plot of land, which I did not allow, of course. The riri is also done in connection with animals, only by words here, of course. They say, for example: “ A riri lon lelugei enan tugo, it is bound in my heart, this chicken, ” that is, it is my property. Then they also further take the wor d riri with a moral obligation. One says, for example: [Page 135] “U a riri uon enan a[unknown]a[unknown], I am bound to that work, ” that is, I am obliged to do it. In any case the former riri is the root of the latter.' [394] Land could be transferred through barter in the manner of other goods: 'The acquisition of property on Truk is usually done by means of barter. Lands, houses, and canoes are bartered. Smaller units of exchange are sebi (wooden bowls), deig (curcuma), faupar (red shell disks), hip mats, modesty bands, fishing nets, aromatic oils, coconut cord, home-grown tobacco, and sleeping mats. These products of native industry are not present in like quantity or in like quality on all the islands. Thus, Sapesis on Fefan is famous for its fine bowls, Pol for curcuma and oils, Iluk for its fine hip mats.' [395]

♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ Women commonly held property, but husbands and children retained usufruct rights over the property of mothers and wives: 'The right to private property exists among the Truk natives. Everyone of them, be it man or woman, adult or child, can own property, for example, land, houses, cattle, money, etc. Whatever the wife brings into the marriage remains wholly at her disposal.' [396] 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.' [397]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ present ♥ Land was held by both individuals and corporate descent groups: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [398] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [399] Elder brothers held supervision rights over inherited land held by the sib, but land obtained from other sources was the exclusive property of younger siblings: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [400] Males could exclude children from inherited land: 'If a man dies, the lands which belonged to him personally pass on to his children. Such land is called fanuei me uon semei, my land from my father. The particulars of the distribution are left completely to his discretion. He can also exclude one child or another, as he likes. The lands that he has jointly with his brothers, however, are taken by them. It frequently happens that the brothers, in case the children are minors, take all the lands in order to administer them, whereby one piece or another remains in their hands forever. In general the father considers only the children from the present marriage. Children from former marriages, who live with the mother’s relatives, are not considered. For that they would have had to rejoin him again later. The mother’s property too only in part passes to the children, since her brothers and sisters likewise do not want to come out short.' [401]
♠ Partibility ♣ present ♥ Inheritance was partible: 'If a man dies, the lands which belonged to him personally pass on to his children. Such land is called fanuei me uon semei, my land from my father. The particulars of the distribution are left completely to his discretion. He can also exclude one child or another, as he likes. The lands that he has jointly with his brothers, however, are taken by them. It frequently happens that the brothers, in case the children are minors, take all the lands in order to administer them, whereby one piece or another remains in their hands forever. In general the father considers only the children from the present marriage. Children from former marriages, who live with the mother’s relatives, are not considered. For that they would have had to rejoin him again later. The mother’s property too only in part passes to the children, since her brothers and sisters likewise do not want to come out short.' [402]
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ According to Fischer, lease arrangements were an artefact of the colonial period: 'Rent for money is purely a foreign introduction and is still poorly understood on some of the outer islands. On Puluwat recently the owner of the plot on which the sub-dispensary stands was worried about accepting rent from the island. He believed that after a number of years as he gradually received the full value of the land in money his title to the land would inexorably slip away from him. On the other hand, he did want to be paid for the food trees cleared away to prepare the site of the building.' [403] But prior to colonization, senior lineages and dominant clans could grant residential and usufruct rights to junior groups on a provisional basis: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [404] Individuals could also grant provisional land rights to other individuals: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [405] We are unsure whether this constitutes lease according to our Code Book, but have assumed so for the time being.
♠ Lease Rights ♣ absent ♥ There was no state structure that could enforce provisional titles. However, chiefs occasionally confiscated property granted to junior lineages and provisional title holders in case of disobedience (such as failure to deliver food prestations on the occasion of feasts): 'As noted above, the relations between a chief and the people of his district are patterned largely after the relations between the parties in divided ownership. As provisional title holders to territory, the various lineages owe the chief periodic gifts of produce from the land. These gifts are rendered at stated times in connection with feasts given in the chief’s honor. He fixes the time for these feasts in accordance with a seasonal calendar: two feasts during the breadfruit season, and two more during the turmeric season. The men of the various lineages prepare breadfruit in giant wooden bowls while the women fish. Other types of food are also collected. On the day of the feast each lineage brings its food in a procession, the men carrying the giant bowls on frames and chanting as they walk. The food is carried into the meeting house of the chief’s lineage. The chief then selects the largest bowl for himself and his lineage mates and redistributes the rest of the food to the assembled population. The distribution is made according to lineage and is roughly proportional to the size of each lineage. The contributions which each lineage makes are also proportional to the size of its membership and the amount of productive land which it controls. If a lineage fails in this obligation, the chief has the right to confiscate its holdings. Should he indicate dissatisfaction with the contribution of a particular lineage, its members will hasten to prepare additional food and other gifts to mollify him.' [406]
♠ Transfer of property ♣ present ♥ Land was held by both individuals and corporate descent groups: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [407] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [408] Land was heritable: 'If a man dies, the lands which belonged to him personally pass on to his children. Such land is called fanuei me uon semei, my land from my father. The particulars of the distribution are left completely to his discretion. He can also exclude one child or another, as he likes. The lands that he has jointly with his brothers, however, are taken by them. It frequently happens that the brothers, in case the children are minors, take all the lands in order to administer them, whereby one piece or another remains in their hands forever. In general the father considers only the children from the present marriage. Children from former marriages, who live with the mother’s relatives, are not considered. For that they would have had to rejoin him again later. The mother’s property too only in part passes to the children, since her brothers and sisters likewise do not want to come out short.' [409] Land could be transferred through barter in the manner of other goods: 'The acquisition of property on Truk is usually done by means of barter. Lands, houses, and canoes are bartered. Smaller units of exchange are sebi (wooden bowls), deig (curcuma), faupar (red shell disks), hip mats, modesty bands, fishing nets, aromatic oils, coconut cord, home-grown tobacco, and sleeping mats. These products of native industry are not present in like quantity or in like quality on all the islands. Thus, Sapesis on Fefan is famous for its fine bowls, Pol for curcuma and oils, Iluk for its fine hip mats.' [410] Both men and women commonly bought and sold property: 'The position of the Truk woman in legal respects is not degrading. They are not weakly in the hands of the husband. I have seen men who were terribly henpecked. The women have their property, are able to acquire, buy, and sell without the husband’s consent. The children also belong exclusively to them. They have little work and for the most part are treated well, even if only to prevent them from running away. It is considered wrong to beat women.' [411] 'The right to private property exists among the Truk natives. Everyone of them, be it man or woman, adult or child, can own property, for example, land, houses, cattle, money, etc. Whatever the wife brings into the marriage remains wholly at her disposal.' [412] 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.' [413] Property acquired through barter was treated differently from property acquired through inheritance: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [414]
♠ Use of property ♣ absent ♥ We have found no evidence for specific uses of property poscribed for land owned by individuals.
♠ Property market ♣ present ♥ While land could be transferred freely through either barter or inheritance, we are unsure whether this constitutes a 'property market', as no state was present prior to colonization. We have coded 'present' for the time being, but this remains in need of confirmation.

♠ Other property ownership ♣ present ♥ 'The right to private property exists among the Truk natives. Everyone of them, be it man or woman, adult or child, can own property, for example, land, houses, cattle, money, etc. Whatever the wife brings into the marriage remains wholly at her disposal.' [415]

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ present ♥ Inheritance was partible: 'If a man dies, the lands which belonged to him personally pass on to his children. Such land is called fanuei me uon semei, my land from my father. The particulars of the distribution are left completely to his discretion. He can also exclude one child or another, as he likes. The lands that he has jointly with his brothers, however, are taken by them. It frequently happens that the brothers, in case the children are minors, take all the lands in order to administer them, whereby one piece or another remains in their hands forever. In general the father considers only the children from the present marriage. Children from former marriages, who live with the mother’s relatives, are not considered. For that they would have had to rejoin him again later. The mother’s property too only in part passes to the children, since her brothers and sisters likewise do not want to come out short.' [416]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ Land held by corporate descent groups was transmitted matrilineally, but fathers also transmitted land to their own children: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [417] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [418] 'If a man dies, the lands which belonged to him personally pass on to his children. Such land is called fanuei me uon semei, my land from my father. The particulars of the distribution are left completely to his discretion. He can also exclude one child or another, as he likes. The lands that he has jointly with his brothers, however, are taken by them. It frequently happens that the brothers, in case the children are minors, take all the lands in order to administer them, whereby one piece or another remains in their hands forever. In general the father considers only the children from the present marriage. Children from former marriages, who live with the mother’s relatives, are not considered. For that they would have had to rejoin him again later. The mother’s property too only in part passes to the children, since her brothers and sisters likewise do not want to come out short.' [419]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ Land held by corporate descent groups was transmitted matrilineally, but fathers also transmitted land to their own children: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [420] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [421] Husbands and children retained usufruct rights over land held by mothers and wives; sharing and cooperation within the sib was common: 'The right to private property exists among the Truk natives. Everyone of them, be it man or woman, adult or child, can own property, for example, land, houses, cattle, money, etc. Whatever the wife brings into the marriage remains wholly at her disposal.' [422] 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.' [423] Elder brothes held supervision rights over property owned by the sib: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [424] The presence of both individually-held property and matrilineal descent groups affected the treatment of womens' property after their passing: 'If a man dies, the lands which belonged to him personally pass on to his children. Such land is called fanuei me uon semei, my land from my father. The particulars of the distribution are left completely to his discretion. He can also exclude one child or another, as he likes. The lands that he has jointly with his brothers, however, are taken by them. It frequently happens that the brothers, in case the children are minors, take all the lands in order to administer them, whereby one piece or another remains in their hands forever. In general the father considers only the children from the present marriage. Children from former marriages, who live with the mother’s relatives, are not considered. For that they would have had to rejoin him again later. The mother’s property too only in part passes to the children, since her brothers and sisters likewise do not want to come out short.' [425]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ absent ♥ Elder brothers held supervision rights over property owned by the sib: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [426] However, this does not amount to primogeniture inheritance.
♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ absent ♥
♠ other inheritance practices ♣ present ♥ Corporate descent groups held land transmitted through matrilineal inheritance: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [427] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [428] Elder brothers held supervision rights over property owned by the sib: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [429] Cooperation and sharing within the sib was common: 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.' [430]
♠ Trusts ♣ absent ♥
♠ State expropriation ♣ absent ♥
♠ Non-state expropriation ♣ absent ♥

Equity[edit]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ {absent; present} ♥ The Chuukese were organized according to membership in matrilineal clans: 'Chuuk's population is divided into a number of dispersed, matrilineal clans. Within any one district the several lineages are usually but not always of different clans. There are also personal kindreds. As a principle of clan and lineage membership, descent is matrilineal, but otherwise kinship is reckoned bilaterally.' [431] 'The domestic unit was an extended family, based on the women of a lineage or sublineage. It consisted of at least one experienced older woman and two more younger women of childbearing age together with their husbands. Unmarried sons and brothers slept apart in their lineage's meeting house. Extended family households continued through the periods of foreign administration.' [432] According to Goodenough, levirate/sororate marriages were practiced: 'Marriage took place for women a bit before and for men a bit after the age of 20. Premarital courtship was covert and included sexual relations. Residence was usually matrilocal. Divorce, common before the birth of children, was rare thereafter. It could be initiated by either spouse and was often instigated by the wife's brothers. Levirate and sororate remarriages were valued. To keep soil and trees that had been granted to the children of a lineage's men circulating back, marriage preferences with one or another kind of cousin, varying from locality to locality, were widespread.' [433] According to Bollig, polygyny was fairly widespread, partly as a result of levirate/sororate marriages: 'Polygamy (1) 1 a akaruu, he has two wives; a akaulu[unknown]at, he has three wives, etc. is very widespread on Truk. Men with two to nine wives are numerous. One becomes polygamous through the death of his brother, for out of brotherly love he has an obligation to take the widow for himself (levirate marriage). Another one marries a widow with daughters and takes the latter, as soon as they are old enough, as secondary wives. A third takes the sisters of his wife for himself, which is the case for most polygamous situations. The reasons for polygamy vary. One wants to keep landed property together by this means, or to concentrate it in his hands; another wants to have workers, since the secondary wives fish for him. Strangely enough, the motive forces for polygamy are said to be mostly the women themselves. They offer their daughters, sisters, [Page 109] and nieces to their husbands as secondary wives, in order that no strange family come in. This happens especially if they have no children, for if a strange woman comes in and gives birth to children, they will be pushed aside. They try to prevent this by means of the above measure. If polygamy has come about in this way, a peaceful life together is possible, for the women stay together in their own interests. But if the women come from different families or tribes, living together is out of the question. They live apart, and the man has the pleasant task of going back and forth. Unpleasantness and jealousy between the rivals are the order of the day. First one and then the other thinks she is being slighted. First one is tired, then the other. And the husband must always be on the move in order to keep everybody happy. Some polygamous situations which I knew were changing constantly. At one time the husband had four, then some ran away, later they increased to six, etc. A somewhat decent family life is inconceivable. The eventual /101/ children always go with the mother, but the father knows the means for drawing them to him when they are older, so that he will have a work force. Some polygamists are very generous with their wives. They are quickly ready to hand them over to their unmarried relatives, because it gives them advantages. They are able to take a new one and still consider the old one indirectly as their own.' [434] Bollig claims that polyandry was rare: 'Polyandry seems to be rarer. I knew of only one case, in which two brothers had a common wife.' [435] But Caughey claims that while polygamy was present, monogamy was the norm for most: 'In the traditional society polygynous marriages rarely occurred. Since marriage sets up heavy obligations to the wife’s lineage, most men did not want to increase this burden, especially by marrying into different lineages. Interpreted differently, the same reasons provided the rationale for those few cases of polygny that did occur. A man who married more than one wife demonstrated his importance and power. It showed that he was capable of engaging in burdensome social relationships with more than one lineage or that he was able to turn this subordinate relationship into one where he dominated. A famous man of bravery on Fáánakkar was once married at the same time to five wives from four different lineages. Later, in a grand gesture of kindness, he gave one of them away to a wifeless brother.' [436] Expert confirmation is needed on the matter.
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ The classification of variables here is slightly misleading. Polygamy was practiced among both chiefs and followers according to some writers, while others claim that polygyny was associated with greater wealth and power (see above).
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ absent ♥
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ absent ♥
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ ♥ The classification of variables is slightly misleading here as some writers claim that extramarital sexual relations were somewhat common. Goodenough provides a general description of courtship and sexual relations: 'Marriage took place for women a bit before and for men a bit after the age of 20. Premarital courtship was covert and included sexual relations. Residence was usually matrilocal. Divorce, common before the birth of children, was rare thereafter. It could be initiated by either spouse and was often instigated by the wife's brothers. Levirate and sororate remarriages were valued. To keep soil and trees that had been granted to the children of a lineage's men circulating back, marriage preferences with one or another kind of cousin, varying from locality to locality, were widespread.' [437] According to Goodenough, premarital sexual relations were common: 'LIKE many other Oceanic peoples, the natives of Truk allow their adolescents considerable freedom in sexual matters. There is no stigma of any kind attached to young men or women who have sexual relations before marriage. No value whatsoever is attributed to virginity in girls or boys. All informants agree that girls normally start having intercourse when, at about the age of fourteen, their breasts become fairly well developed. The initial sexual experiences of boys occur at a somewhat later age, between the ages of sixteen and seventeen.' [438] Bollig claims that 'licentious' behavior was common and tolerated: 'Inemes, the Venus of the Truk people, has to be mentioned separately, because her cult is very different in character from that of the other spirits. She is the protectress of lechery, and everything that goes with it is dedicated to her. None of the gods and spirits has as extensive a circle of worshipers as Inemes. This fact alone is proof of the low moral position of the Truk people, who know no distinction between licit and illicit sexual intercourse and who, despite the oloudd have no conception of chastity and even less of virginity.' [439] 'There is another detestable custom which likewise derives its origin from Inemes. It is the puanelö or nadelö (1). A number of young fellows get together and announce their licentiousness. They hang up the faren I[unknown]emes, decorate it with wreaths, necklaces, etc., paint themselves yellow from head to foot, and roam about to find companions in their lechery. Women and girls who do not want to co-operate are sprinkled with “magic oil” so that their hair falls out and ugly wounds and boils appear.' [440] 'During this time also moonlight dances in honor of Inemes are performed. They are the notorious ouönu dances.' [441] 'It is not surprising that so many marriages break up as a consequence of these ouönu dances. The licentiousness of the women is worst during this time. Since it is forbidden to pick coconuts, they lie in wait for the men so as to catch any in the act of violating the prohibition. If they have happily found one, they tear his clothes, scratch him, take away his food, etc. That is the detestable worship of Inemes, a dark point in the lives of the Truk natives, who otherwise are so congenial.' [442] 'Jealousy among the women is in general not very great. It is worse among some men, who on account of it treat their wives like slaves. Wherever the wives go, they accompany them. They never make long trips, in order always to be able to be home at night. At the slightest suspicion, they beat their wives. Men of this type are not popular. Their jealousy is smiled at pityingly by the more liberal-minded. It is worthless, for the women are slier than the men in this respect. A word, a glance, a movement, a dropped stone, the flashing of a mirror reveals the intentions of the faithless one to her lover, without the neighborhood noticing anything. Even the harmless smoking is used in reaching an understanding. The woman rubs the tip of the cigarette with some aromatic oil and hands it to the man she desires to win. He immediately tastes the oil and knows what the woman wants of him. Thus, there are innumerable ways of reaching an understanding between those who have bad intentions. This refined system of adultery is not surprising, however, in the case of a people among whom Inemes, the goddess of lechery, swings her scepter.' [443] Writing in the colonial period, Gladwin claims that children conceived through adultery were integrated into matrilineal kin groups without much consternation: 'Another aspect of kinship deserves mention. As we have seen this is a matrilineal society, which means that the social placement of a child in the lineage and wider social groupings depends in most respects upon his tie with his mother. The Trukese take this quite literally and therefore pay little or no attention to who may [Page 51] be the child’s actual biological father, even though this may be perfectly obvious. While the unmarried mother may find herself in a somewhat embarrassing social position, as far as can be determined no stigma whatsoever attaches to the child. Children of adulterous unions similarly are simply considered to be the children of the man to whom their mother was married at the time of their birth. Perhaps most important of the problems resolved by this device is that of children fathered by foreigners. At the present time there are literally hundreds of children whose appearance unmistakably shows them to have had Japanese fathers, and who in another society could readily form a class of half-breeds or mestizos with highly disruptive effects for themselves and the society as a whole. But on Truk they are simply Trukese children, treated no differently from any others, and properly furnished with mothers and fathers. Those who have seen the deep cleavage formed in societies elsewhere by mestizo groups can readily appreciate the degree of the good fortune of the Trukese in this regard. There are a few true half-castes on Truk, descendents of early traders who married Trukese women and raised their children themselves; these people consider themselves half-castes and are treated as such. But because this limited group can expand only slightly, not being augmented by further foreign transgressions, they can have little harmful effect and, in fact, have used the business knowledge passed down to them from their fathers or grandfathers to make major contributions to the economic adjustment of Truk under American administration.' [444] It is difficult to quantify a 'typical number of extramarital partners' from these descriptions.


Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ present ♥ Chuukese followed a gendered division of labour: 'Traditionally, men gardened, cooked and processed food in bulk (in the earth oven), did deep-water fishing, engaged in war and public affairs, and practiced the arts of canoe and house building and of wood, shell, and stone working. Women wove, plaited mats, prepared meals (as distinct from food in bulk), did inshore fishing, and took main responsibility for child care. Men and women have both entered into school teaching, clerical work, and administration.' [445] Women were excluded from some ceremonial activities due to gendered ritual taboos: 'A few dances are also connected with the worship of the spirits. These are the dugia (dance with kurukur, that is, staffs of orange wood) and the älimod, a dance which is performed in a sitting position. The former is a men’s dance; the latter may be performed by both sexes. During the entire dancing period the dancers are shut up in the udd. This is surrounded by a fence which no unbidden person, especially no woman, may approach. No indecent word occurs in these spirit dances, in contrast to the other dances. They say that the spirits do not like indecent things in connection with the men, a statement which does not correspond with their views on other occasions.' [446] 'The pue is handled in different ways. The most important and solemn pue is the girot. The soupue makes the girot in the udd in the /66/ presence of his pupils. Women are not allowed to watch. As a matter of fact no woman is allowed to make any kind of pue.' [447] 'After the apu[unknown]etou, the canoe, which is called faremai (= önumai) if it is a rowing canoe, is tested. It goes out on the sea for the kitir (rowing or sailing race). No woman is allowed to board the faremai, and no food may be put in it. The food must go with the other canoes. In the center of the new canoe sits the souatomai, who caresses the canoe and invokes the spirits. Having reached water favorable for navigation, the crew starts to row, and the vessels shoot across the broad surface with the speed of arrows. The invocations of the suatomai become more and more animated; the iéfot, iéfot of the rowers, wilder and wilder. Strange /74/ canoes passing by must be careful about coming too close to the faremai, for it is sacred.' [448] Men in turn were excluded from some female-gendered activities, such as childbirth: 'It is not so simple for a child to be born on Truk. One can call it a near miracle if a child escapes all who struggle for its life before and after birth. The woman in childbirth is separated from the other occupants of the house. She often occupies a little house built especially for the birth. One or another older woman from among the relatives, called soufam or souetikar (midwives), is to be found with her. Entrance is forbidden to all men.' [449] According to Caughey, lineage leaders were usually male and brothers had authority over sisters: 'A lineage is conceived of as a group of siblings ( pwiipwi). In a group of real siblings, males have authority over females and the “eldest brother” ( mwaaniichi) is the leader. Within the lineage, the oldest male of the oldest generation is usually the “lineage leader” ( mmwen noon eterekes, [Page 65] “first inside of the lineage”).' [450] According to Bollig, brothers were owed respect by sisters: 'As long as the children are small, they run around completely naked. When they become bigger and approach maturity, the boys receive their akadöwar (modesty band), the girls their atok (hip mat). From the day when the boy has his band he is considered a youth. The sister, when meeting him, must aporo, that is, bow. An old custom to indicate the age of a boy is connected with this modesty band. To the question, “How old is the boy?” comes the answer, for example, “ etö efärin” (= akadöwar), that is, a modesty band, which means that he has grown beyond the age of a boy. The natives do not have any precise estimate of their age. None of them is able [Page 99] to say how old he is with any exactness. To designate the stages of age, they have mönukol (1) 1 Literally: the conceived. , sediben (2) 2 “It has no will.” semirid (3) 3 “It cannot think.” = child, that is, very small children. For somewhat bigger ones, the expressions äd, säri (boy), le[unknown]in (girl) are used; at maturity, whether married or not, öluöl (youth), fabul (young woman). Adult persons are called muan (man), fefin (woman).' [451] However, women could hold property even without the husband's consent (Chuukese lineages were matrilineal): 'The position of the Truk woman in legal respects is not degrading. They are not weakly in the hands of the husband. I have seen men who were terribly henpecked. The women have their property, are able to acquire, buy, and sell without the husband’s consent. The children also belong exclusively to them. They have little work and for the most part are treated well, even if only to prevent them from running away. It is considered wrong to beat women.' [452] We are unsure whether the above examples constitute differences in 'legal rights', but have assumed so for the time being.
♠ Social class ♣ present ♥ As a clan-based society, the Chuukese were stratified according to lineage rather than social class: 'Chuuk's population is divided into a number of dispersed, matrilineal clans. Within any one district the several lineages are usually but not always of different clans. There are also personal kindreds. As a principle of clan and lineage membership, descent is matrilineal, but otherwise kinship is reckoned bilaterally.' [453] 'The domestic unit was an extended family, based on the women of a lineage or sublineage. It consisted of at least one experienced older woman and two more younger women of childbearing age together with their husbands. Unmarried sons and brothers slept apart in their lineage's meeting house. Extended family households continued through the periods of foreign administration.' [454] Chiefly status was tied to lineages in full ownership of territorial districts: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [455] Male chiefs were attached to the senior lineages of matrilineal descent groups: A district chiefship was divided between the oldest man in the senior female line in the chiefly lineage and the oldest man in the lineage generally. The latter was executive chief, or "chief of talk," and the former symbolic chief, or "chief of food." Food presentations were made to the symbolic chief. Sometimes the symbolic and executive functions fell to the same individual; often they did not. The symbolic chief was surrounded by his lineage brothers and by his sons, who acted as his agents. These followers and his sisters and daughters were of chiefly rank, distinct from commoners. Through conquest, a lineage might gain the chiefship in more than one district and establish a junior branch as the chiefly lineage in the conquered district. The now subordinate district rendered food presentations to the superordinate one. Most districts were linked in two rival leagues based on competing schools of magic and ritual relating to war, politics, and rhetoric. A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [456] Bollig claims that members of chiefly lineages received preferential treatment in judicial matters due to nepotism: 'Another weak point of the chiefs is partiality. Members of the chief’s kin group, blood relatives, and friends are not readily punished, not even for serious crimes. On the other hand, poor devils whom the chief cannot bear are sentenced to hard labor for the slightest reason or are reported to the government as “disobedient ones” or “rebels.” In the old days the Truk chiefs did away with troublesome persons by means of the knife; today they do it by means of lies and false denunciations.' [457] We are unsure whether the above examples constitute different 'legal rights' stratified according to 'social class', but have assumed so for the time being.
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ ♥ Writing in the colonial period, Gladwin claims that 'mixed-race' individuals were readily integrated into matrilineal kin groups: 'Another aspect of kinship deserves mention. As we have seen this is a matrilineal society, which means that the social placement of a child in the lineage and wider social groupings depends in most respects upon his tie with his mother. The Trukese take this quite literally and therefore pay little or no attention to who may [Page 51] be the child’s actual biological father, even though this may be perfectly obvious. While the unmarried mother may find herself in a somewhat embarrassing social position, as far as can be determined no stigma whatsoever attaches to the child. Children of adulterous unions similarly are simply considered to be the children of the man to whom their mother was married at the time of their birth. Perhaps most important of the problems resolved by this device is that of children fathered by foreigners. At the present time there are literally hundreds of children whose appearance unmistakably shows them to have had Japanese fathers, and who in another society could readily form a class of half-breeds or mestizos with highly disruptive effects for themselves and the society as a whole. But on Truk they are simply Trukese children, treated no differently from any others, and properly furnished with mothers and fathers. Those who have seen the deep cleavage formed in societies elsewhere by mestizo groups can readily appreciate the degree of the good fortune of the Trukese in this regard. There are a few true half-castes on Truk, descendents of early traders who married Trukese women and raised their children themselves; these people consider themselves half-castes and are treated as such. But because this limited group can expand only slightly, not being augmented by further foreign transgressions, they can have little harmful effect and, in fact, have used the business knowledge passed down to them from their fathers or grandfathers to make major contributions to the economic adjustment of Truk under American administration.' [458] We have not found material indicating the permanent presence of non-Chuukese 'ethnic' or 'racial' groups in the period immediately preceding colonization. The material collected on warfare variables (see above) indicates that captives were massacred or released rather than permanently integrated into Chuukese society. Accordingly, this variable may not be relevant to the pre-colonial period.

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ ♥ Note: Given the absence of a state structure prior to colonization, most of the below variables are irrelevant. Accordingly, only the property variables were coded for the time being.
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to both males and females ♣ ♥
♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ ♥
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ present ♥ Land was held by both individuals and matrilineal descent groups: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [459] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [460] Women commonly held property, but husbands and children retained usufruct rights over the property of mothers and wives: 'The position of the Truk woman in legal respects is not degrading. They are not weakly in the hands of the husband. I have seen men who were terribly henpecked. The women have their property, are able to acquire, buy, and sell without the husband’s consent. The children also belong exclusively to them. They have little work and for the most part are treated well, even if only to prevent them from running away. It is considered wrong to beat women.' [461] 'The right to private property exists among the Truk natives. Everyone of them, be it man or woman, adult or child, can own property, for example, land, houses, cattle, money, etc. Whatever the wife brings into the marriage remains wholly at her disposal.' [462] 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.' [463] However, elder brothers usually held supervision rights over property owned by the sib: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [464] The differentiation between individual and communal property and the practice of matriliny affected the transmission of property by men and women: 'If a man dies, the lands which belonged to him personally pass on to his children. Such land is called fanuei me uon semei, my land from my father. The particulars of the distribution are left completely to his discretion. He can also exclude one child or another, as he likes. The lands that he has jointly with his brothers, however, are taken by them. It frequently happens that the brothers, in case the children are minors, take all the lands in order to administer them, whereby one piece or another remains in their hands forever. In general the father considers only the children from the present marriage. Children from former marriages, who live with the mother’s relatives, are not considered. For that they would have had to rejoin him again later. The mother’s property too only in part passes to the children, since her brothers and sisters likewise do not want to come out short.' [465]

Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ ♥
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ ♥
♠ Education is available to people from all social classes ♣ ♥
♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ Land was held by both individuals and matrilineal descent groups: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [466] 'Individually-owned property was inherited by the owner's children. Corporately-owned property was inherited by the corporation's 'children' (the children of its men) when the corporation's membership died out.' [467] Chiefly lineages owned lands associated with their territorial districts, but could grant usufruct and residential rights to junior lineages on a provisional basis: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [468] Sharing and cooperation within the matrilineal sib was common: 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.' [469] Elder brothers held supervision rights over property owned by the sib: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.' [470] Rights in land itself were differentiated from rights in plantings: 'The Truk people are familiar with a distinction of the ground from what is planted on it. Thus, somebody may possess a piece of land, but the trees that stand on it belong to other people. They or their parents once planted the trees on his property, which is permissible according to Truk customs. If the owners of the trees are dishonest, they need only clear the field beginning from the trees, continue to cultivate, and the poor owner is dispossessed. What confused situations that causes in the case of an eventual transfer of the piece of land to another is easily understandable. I myself have had difficulties for years, since certain people still held on to “their” trees after the piece of land had long been the property of the mission. Only the felling of the trees put an end to all claims.' [471] According to Bollig, land disputes were common: 'The land rights of the Truk people are not based on justice, but on the right of the stronger, the cleverer. There is hardly a plot of land with a clear legal history, and for that reason there are the constant assertions of legal claims, the eternal lawsuits over land. The boldest and most cunning persons always have the best land on Truk. Pretenses for bringing legal claims to foreign property before the chief are easily found in the confused conditions. And once they are found, the Kanakas’ inexhaustible power of invention and art of lying take care of making them convincing to others. That briberies of chiefs, interpreters, and witnesses play a tremendous part in this is obvious, considering the character of the Truk people. No means is too wicked for them in order to “profit. ”' [472] Bollig attributes this issue to land exchanges and vague boundaries: 'A source of land disputes is the exchange of lands. For example: Soni and Soledon exchange two pieces of land. Soni now cultivates his land, while Soledon lets it lie fallow. Soledon dies. His children, who are not on good terms with Soni, cancel the exchange and take possession of the productive piece of land as property of their deceased father. Soni resists. The parties quarrel for years. Sometimes the one wins, sometimes the other, but no one yields.' [473] 'The boundaries of the lands are very vague. They change continually under the hands of both neighbors. Sometimes the one, sometimes the other one, goes beyond them. Then they quarrel. What A has planted B tears out, with the argument that it is already on his territory. What B has planted A tears out, with the same argument. Now which of the two is right?' [474] 'With such uncertain legal conditions it is not at all surprising that quarrels over land are daily events on Truk. Every death, every divorce, every new marriage has a coil of rats’ tails in quarrels as a result. The boundless egotism of the Truk natives is responsible for all this. Everybody wants to have as much as possible. A Trukese can quarrel for years about a tree, a plant, or a foot of land, even though he has plenty of trees that are not producing anything because of lack of care. The German government gave the natives land documents on which the owner, along with all his pieces of land, was recorded. /123/ The hopes placed on this have not been fulfilled. Today everything is as confused as before.' [475] It is unclear whether these disputes were endemic even before colonization or a consequence thereof. Bollig also describes strategies for claiming and asserting rights over land and plantings: 'The natives try to document their right of ownership to pieces of land by clearing something on it from time to time, or they build huts on the individual pieces of land, which they then inhabit temporarily. Dying fathers give their children directions to bury them on their pieces of land so that their grave might thus be proof of ownership for the future. One can often hear in the kobu[unknown] that this is precisely what the people refer to.' [476] 'The riri, that is, to bind, forms a strange title deed on the Truk Islands. For example, somebody sees a tree which bears many fruits. Since he would like some too, he takes the ubud of a coconut palm and wraps it around a branch of the tree in order to make his intention known publicly. In many cases the owner resists such oppression and tears off the ubud. But also in many cases he keeps quiet in order to avoid bigger quarrels.' [477] 'I myself have had experiences with the riri in that the girls “bound” pineapples for themselves on my plot of land, which I did not allow, of course. The riri is also done in connection with animals, only by words here, of course. They say, for example: “ A riri lon lelugei enan tugo, it is bound in my heart, this chicken, ” that is, it is my property. Then they also further take the wor d riri with a moral obligation. One says, for example: [Page 135] “U a riri uon enan a[unknown]a[unknown], I am bound to that work, ” that is, I am obliged to do it. In any case the former riri is the root of the latter.' [478] A wide variety of goods could be transferred through barter: 'The acquisition of property on Truk is usually done by means of barter. Lands, houses, and canoes are bartered. Smaller units of exchange are sebi (wooden bowls), deig (curcuma), faupar (red shell disks), hip mats, modesty bands, fishing nets, aromatic oils, coconut cord, home-grown tobacco, and sleeping mats. These products of native industry are not present in like quantity or in like quality on all the islands. Thus, Sapesis on Fefan is famous for its fine bowls, Pol for curcuma and oils, Iluk for its fine hip mats.' [479] While rights in land were unevenly distributed, the above examples demonstrate that everyone could own and exchange property.


Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥
♠ Education is available to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥
♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥ This variable may not be applicable as we have found no material indicating the permanent presence of non-Chuukese 'ethnic' or 'racial' groups prior to colonization (see above).

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ As a clan-based society, the Chuukese were stratified according to lineages: 'Chuuk's population is divided into a number of dispersed, matrilineal clans. Within any one district the several lineages are usually but not always of different clans. There are also personal kindreds. As a principle of clan and lineage membership, descent is matrilineal, but otherwise kinship is reckoned bilaterally.' [480] 'The domestic unit was an extended family, based on the women of a lineage or sublineage. It consisted of at least one experienced older woman and two more younger women of childbearing age together with their husbands. Unmarried sons and brothers slept apart in their lineage's meeting house. Extended family households continued through the periods of foreign administration.' [481] Chiefly lineages owned land associated with their territorial district: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [482] Chiefs generally hailed from senior lineages and relied on the support of male kin: 'A district chiefship was divided between the oldest man in the senior female line in the chiefly lineage and the oldest man in the lineage generally. The latter was executive chief, or "chief of talk," and the former symbolic chief, or "chief of food." Food presentations were made to the symbolic chief. Sometimes the symbolic and executive functions fell to the same individual; often they did not. The symbolic chief was surrounded by his lineage brothers and by his sons, who acted as his agents. These followers and his sisters and daughters were of chiefly rank, distinct from commoners. Through conquest, a lineage might gain the chiefship in more than one district and establish a junior branch as the chiefly lineage in the conquered district. The now subordinate district rendered food presentations to the superordinate one. Most districts were linked in two rival leagues based on competing schools of magic and ritual relating to war, politics, and rhetoric. A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [483]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ absent ♥
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ absent ♥ As a clan-based society, the Chuukese were stratified according to lineages: 'Chuuk's population is divided into a number of dispersed, matrilineal clans. Within any one district the several lineages are usually but not always of different clans. There are also personal kindreds. As a principle of clan and lineage membership, descent is matrilineal, but otherwise kinship is reckoned bilaterally.' [484] 'The domestic unit was an extended family, based on the women of a lineage or sublineage. It consisted of at least one experienced older woman and two more younger women of childbearing age together with their husbands. Unmarried sons and brothers slept apart in their lineage's meeting house. Extended family households continued through the periods of foreign administration.' [485] Chiefly lineages owned land associated with their territorial district: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [486] Chiefs generally hailed from senior lineages and relied on the support of male kin: 'A district chiefship was divided between the oldest man in the senior female line in the chiefly lineage and the oldest man in the lineage generally. The latter was executive chief, or "chief of talk," and the former symbolic chief, or "chief of food." Food presentations were made to the symbolic chief. Sometimes the symbolic and executive functions fell to the same individual; often they did not. The symbolic chief was surrounded by his lineage brothers and by his sons, who acted as his agents. These followers and his sisters and daughters were of chiefly rank, distinct from commoners. Through conquest, a lineage might gain the chiefship in more than one district and establish a junior branch as the chiefly lineage in the conquered district. The now subordinate district rendered food presentations to the superordinate one. Most districts were linked in two rival leagues based on competing schools of magic and ritual relating to war, politics, and rhetoric. A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [487] However, it appears that the differentiation between chiefly and commoner lineages did not amount to a formal caste system: 'There was little clothing differentiation according to status. Chiefs and men of wealth wore somewhat more richly ornamented garments, but these statuses were not associated with special items of clothing. This privilege was reserved for the specialist in native lore (itang) and for the breadfruit summoner, souattomei. On those occasions when the itang talked to the people in the meeting house, he wore a special cloak (nite), woven from banana fibers along the same pattern as the ordinary man’s cloak, pinu; at these times also, he was the only person allowed to wear the conical pandanus hat, saaim. The souattomei possessed a special loincloth (meciijenimei) which [Page 323] he wore during the model canoe races connected with the breadfruit summoning ceremonies, and also at the feast signalling the end of these ceremonies.' [488]

♠ social mobility ♣ absent ♥ As a clan-based society, the Chuukese were stratified according to lineages: 'Chuuk's population is divided into a number of dispersed, matrilineal clans. Within any one district the several lineages are usually but not always of different clans. There are also personal kindreds. As a principle of clan and lineage membership, descent is matrilineal, but otherwise kinship is reckoned bilaterally.'
