[489] 'The population is divided into a number of matrilineal, exogamous sibs which function chiefly in regulating marriage. More important from a functional viewpoint are matrilineal lineages, the members of which trace common descent in the female line from a remembered ancestress. Lineages were, until recently, localized in hamlets; they own land, and the members of a lineage (or a descent line within a lineage) form the usual cooperative work group. Lineage (district) chiefs constituted the highest political authority in most cases.' [490] 'The domestic unit was an extended family, based on the women of a lineage or sublineage. It consisted of at least one experienced older woman and two more younger women of childbearing age together with their husbands. Unmarried sons and brothers slept apart in their lineage's meeting house. Extended family households continued through the periods of foreign administration.' [491] Chiefly lineages owned land associated with their territorial district: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [492] Chiefs generally hailed from senior lineages and relied on the support of male kin: 'A district chiefship was divided between the oldest man in the senior female line in the chiefly lineage and the oldest man in the lineage generally. The latter was executive chief, or "chief of talk," and the former symbolic chief, or "chief of food." Food presentations were made to the symbolic chief. Sometimes the symbolic and executive functions fell to the same individual; often they did not. The symbolic chief was surrounded by his lineage brothers and by his sons, who acted as his agents. These followers and his sisters and daughters were of chiefly rank, distinct from commoners. Through conquest, a lineage might gain the chiefship in more than one district and establish a junior branch as the chiefly lineage in the conquered district. The now subordinate district rendered food presentations to the superordinate one. Most districts were linked in two rival leagues based on competing schools of magic and ritual relating to war, politics, and rhetoric. A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [493] Accordingly, we have assumed little to no social mobility between chiefly and commoner lineages.

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ equal ♥ The population was stratified according to lineages: 'The Truk people are divided into hundreds of tribes and tribelets, whose number of members exhibits the greatest dissimilarities. There are some who number scarcely 10 and some who count 100 to 200 members. The most varied kin-groups live together in such a tribe. Thus, the tribe of the Sabu people on the island of Iluk, amounting to about 120 persons, includes members of the kin groups Witö, Atau, Soborelo[unknown], Ipä[unknown]es, Sobu, [Page 124] and Fesinim, some of whom came into the tribe through adoption and some through marriage. The members of a tribe usually live together in irregularly laid out, small communities. Only a few live in the mountains by themselves.' [494] Chiefs were members of senior lineages: 'Each tribe has its chief ( somol ki), often even two or three. The chiefs, as a rule, are taken from the kin group which was the dominant one from the beginning and perhaps first settled on the land concerned. Thus, on Uman the Fesinim kin group rules; on Poloas, Immo; and on Fefan, Soufar. The Pue kin group rose in this island and occupies many a chief’s seat. Atau governs on Faituk, Sepelo on Udot, after Ipä[unknown]es perished in the wars, and Sapulupi on Wöla. In all there might be 400 to 500 chiefs. If one considers that the total population of the Truk lagoon hardly amounts to 12,000 people, the number of chiefs must be designated as unnaturally large. In spite of the fact that one such chief has a small area with few people under him, compactness and co-operation are missing. Although the chief proudly calls himself awen fanu, mouth of the land, lugen fanu, heart of the land, or säli[unknown]en fanu, ear of the land, he generally has little to say. Only some one or other understands how to keep his people together, because he knows how to gain influence as a result of previous warlike deeds or personal cunning and lack of consideration. In addition to this, several who live in his territory come from other tribes, pay tribute to their homeland chief, and do not concern themselves about him.' [495] They were succeeded by brothers and brothers' sons: 'There is always provision for a successor for a deceased chief. The children are not qualified to inherit, but eo ipso his brothers, and, to be sure, according to age. If there are no longer any brothers, then come the children of the brothers. It is said of somebody who has improperly become chief that he is not popun somol ( popun = tribe), that is, legitimate chief.' [496] 'When a chief becomes old and weak, he usually has a younger person at his side who performs the more difficult tasks. The old chief is then called somobun mo[unknown]ö, chief of the food, because the first fruits are still delivered to him. The young chief, who has the right of succession, receives the title somobun kabas, chief of speech.' [497] We have provisionally treated senior lineages as 'high nobility' as defined in the Code Book. However, this is open to review and only a vague approximation.
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ absent ♥
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ absent ♥


♠ occupational mobility ♣ absent ♥ Prior to colonization, the Chuukese economy was subsistence-based: 'In the past, swidden gardens with dry taro, turmeric, and sugar cane were few and small. Breadfruit, supplemented by wet taro, was the staple. Being seasonal, breadfruit was preserved by fermenting in pits. Copra has become the only export. Fishing was important.' [498] 'Traditionally, men gardened, cooked and processed food in bulk (in the earth oven), did deep-water fishing, engaged in war and public affairs, and practiced the arts of canoe and house building and of wood, shell, and stone working. Women wove, plaited mats, prepared meals (as distinct from food in bulk), did inshore fishing, and took main responsibility for child care.' [499] 'Traditional crafts included: making outrigger paddle canoes; building houses; woodworking (to make bowls, storage chests, spears); gardening; cordage (to make rope, string, slings); working stone (for sling stones), shell (for adz blades), and coral (for breadfruit pounders); preparing medicines; loom weaving with hibiscus and banana fibers (to make loin clothes, wraparound skirts, shirts, mosquito canopies); plaiting (of baskets, mats); and other leaf working (for thatch, sun hats). Sewing arts and dressmaking have replaced weaving.' [500] Accordingly, occupational mobility does not apply here as a category.

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ 0% ♥ Note: It appears that prisoners of war were usually either executed or released rather than enslaved. It appears that slavery and debt peonage did not exist in the pre-colonial period.

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0% ♥

Types

♠ Chattel slavery ♣ absent ♥
♠ Elite slavery ♣ absent ♥
♠ Bonded serfdom ♣ absent ♥
♠ Corvée labor to a landowner ♣ present ♥ Chiefs were owed first fruits: 'As a reward for his faithful activity, the chief receives the muen, that is, the first fruits from the yields of the fields and trees, for example, breadfruit, bananas, and taro. It is said that many chiefs go to the fields in order to inspect their condition so that if the occasion arises they can check the deliveries. The Truk chiefs do not have any other legal claims. Nevertheless their subjects give them assistance in their own interests. The chief usually receives the best part from festive meals and fishing. If there is fishing with mention of the name of the chief, however, he receives everything, and nobody is allowed to withhold anything. Woe to the one who in former times did not pay his tribute properly. He had no prospect of a long life. It is said of the old chiefs that they always had the blow shell beside them. When the puk, puk of the shell resounded from the chief’s house, the people knew its meaning immediately. They quickly came running in order to /117/ learn the wishes of the tyrant. At one time he would desire kon (pounded breadfruit), at another time fish, then [Page 128] bananas, then coconuts to drink. The chiefs today no longer dare commit these outrages but they still have sufficient means to obtain some things on the side. They announce a dance, for example, or hold an efilul (celebration in connection with house-building, held when the frame is erected). They certainly do not come out short at the feast connected with this. As a matter of fact, most chiefs understand very well how to entice from their people whatever they might like to have.' [501] Junior lineages and provisional title holders owed food prestations to chiefs on the occasion of feasts: 'As noted above, the relations between a chief and the people of his district are patterned largely after the relations between the parties in divided ownership. As provisional title holders to territory, the various lineages owe the chief periodic gifts of produce from the land. These gifts are rendered at stated times in connection with feasts given in the chief’s honor. He fixes the time for these feasts in accordance with a seasonal calendar: two feasts during the breadfruit season, and two more during the turmeric season. The men of the various lineages prepare breadfruit in giant wooden bowls while the women fish. Other types of food are also collected. On the day of the feast each lineage brings its food in a procession, the men carrying the giant bowls on frames and chanting as they walk. The food is carried into the meeting house of the chief’s lineage. The chief then selects the largest bowl for himself and his lineage mates and redistributes the rest of the food to the assembled population. The distribution is made according to lineage and is roughly proportional to the size of each lineage. The contributions which each lineage makes are also proportional to the size of its membership and the amount of productive land which it controls. If a lineage fails in this obligation, the chief has the right to confiscate its holdings. Should he indicate dissatisfaction with the contribution of a particular lineage, its members will hasten to prepare additional food and other gifts to mollify him.' [502]
♠ Corvée labor to the state ♣ absent ♥ There was no state structure. Accordingly, we have coded for chiefs as 'landowners' in this case (see above).

Restraints

♠ Owners can execute slaves ♣ absent ♥
♠ Owners can impose corporal punishment on slaves ♣ absent ♥
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ absent ♥

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ present ♥ According to Bollig, the bodies of slain enemies were cut into pieces and eaten: 'The prisoners were usually done away with.Anyone who had a bone to pick with them cut their throats. Yet I have also heard of cases in which the prisoners were spared on please of the women and were later released. The slain enemies were cut into pieces. /106/ In the frenzy some drank their blood or cut pieces from the soft parts, roasted them, and ate them to cool their vengeance.' [503] He also mentions what appears to be a form of human sacrifice: 'The spirit Paladiu on the island of Falanu is even more modest. Human excrement satisfies him as an offering. During a war human sacrifices seem to have been offered also. At least cases are known where captives had their throats cut over the graves of those who had fallen, so that the blood would be sprinkled over them.' [504] Bollig also claims the ritual mutilation of dead enemies: 'When the plans were ready and the preparations completed, the entire body of troops gathered opposite the enemy territory at a place which was suitable for the battle (rasen moun). Here the ida[unknown] put up the önusoso or soönu (erected spirit). This önusoso was a wooden figure which represented a human being or an animal, for example, shark, hake. In some cases a corpse itself was used. For example, such a soönu was to be found on the premises of the mission on Fautuen. It was the corpse of an enemy warrior who had been treacherously killed. It was set against a tree stump, with the face toward the country of the enemy. The face was terribly disfigured, in that the lips were cut off. The mouth stood open, prevented from closing by a spike of bone. In its hand the corpse held a spear which likewise was directed toward the enemy.' [505] LeBar denies the cannibalization of enemies: 'Informants denied that slain enemies were ever eaten on Truk. A variant way of handling the dead in the old days required that the corpse be wrapped in mats and hung in the house. Relatives might show their fondness for the deceased by eating [Page 223] a bit of the flesh or drinking a few drops of the body fluids collected in a bowl beneath the corpse.' [506] We have provisionally followed Bollig as heprovides more material overall, but expert feedback is needed on the matter..
♠ Low social class ♣ absent ♥
♠ Elites ♣ absent ♥
♠ Retainers ♣ absent ♥
♠ Outsiders ♣ present ♥ See above.

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Eva Brandl; Enrico Cioni ♥ EB coded the vast majority of this data; EC edited this page and changed a small number of codes in order to make them more comparable to ones in other NGAs.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥ The name of the historical or archaeological expert who supervised coding and checked/improved/approved the result. If more than one expert was involved, list all.

Description of the Normative Ideology

Religious beliefs centred on mana and spirit beings: 'In traditional belief, spirit beings were widely distributed in the sky, under the sea, and on land. The important places among spirit lands in the sky were: a region under the dome of Heaven, home of the gods who could take human form; a region in the south from which came all the plant and marine life that gave people food; and a region named 'Achaw' or 'Kachaw,' abode of the ancestors of many Chuukese clans and particularly of the clans associated with the chiefship and the special bodies of magical lore from which chiefly power derived. Spirits could accomplish their intentions at will and were thus the source of all that was MANAMAN (mana), such as efficacious spells, medicines, and rituals. Good souls of the dead were consulted through mediums. The Chuukese also invoked in spells the spirits inhabiting the dome of Heaven, presided over by 'Great Spirit,' and the spirits associated with particular crafts and major bodies of lore.' [507] Illness was attributed to the agency of malevolent spirits: 'Sickness was believed to result from the 'bite' of a malevolent spirit, or of any other spirit one had offended or that was controlled by a ritual specialist one had offended or by a sorcerer. Sickness might also result from soul loss. In all but the latter case, treatment involved the use of medicines to be applied externally, to be drunk, or to be inhaled. For soul loss, a spirit medium was consulted to help find and restore the soul. Divination was used as a diagnostic aid in cases of severe or prolonged illness. Massage was used to treat bruises, local infections, and muscle ailments.' [508] A variety of ritual specialists were active: 'Ritual practices were conducted by their own specialists. They included spirit mediums, breadfruit summoners, fish summoners, healers, masters of spells, masters of sorcery, builders, navigators, diviners, and most importantly the masters of magic and ritual relating to war and politics. Their knowledge was private property passed down to their children and junior lineage mates.' [509] 'Major ceremonies were those associated with death, communicating with good souls of the dead, summoning breadfruit, and making food presentations to chiefs. Ritual was also associated with divination, curing, warfare, political meetings, house building, and courtship.' [510] The Chuuk islands experienced a higher degree of regional ceremonial integration prior to the 18th century (see above under 'preceding quasi-polity'). Whatever information is available on this would have to be covered in an additional data sheet.

Note: We have not found much material on morality and ideology. Accordingly, the below information may be incomplete. As no written records explicating normative ideology were present, values have to be inferred from oral histories and everyday behavior: 'When the people of Fáánakkar talk about character and valuables, they assume certain definitions and rules, but they [Page 160] also typically retell or allude to a set of standard illustrative stories in which the rules are encoded. That is, each value label conjures up in people’s minds a series of basic images embodied in a stock of stories about people who behaved with extraordinary bravery, arrogance, or strong thought. Sometimes these stories are based on the semi-legendary exploits of people in the old days ( e.g., the story of how C.J. killed the outer island sorcerer or the tale about the arrogance of N.P., and how he was stabbed by the wronged chief’s nephew). Other stories involve the dramatic behavior of people in the community today ( e.g., accounts of major boundary disputes, knife fights, sorcery battles, and sumptuous feasts). The situations and events in these stories provide a person with standards for measuring the conduct of other people and guidelines for behaving.' [511]

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ present ♥ According to Goodenough, chiefs were thought to be connected with the sky world, its gods and its mana: 'A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [512]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ present ♥ Chiefs and their associates employed sorcery for coercive purposes: 'There were no police. A chief's brothers or sons might act on his behalf to intimidate or attack someone who had offended him. But it was control of magical power, either by the chief or one his brothers or sons, that made improper conduct liable to punishment. Major craft specialists could also make ill those who violated the taboos of their craft. Finally, members of chiefly lineages and their close associates were likely to have knowledge of sorcery. All such knowledge gave punitive power to chiefs and important specialists. People stressed maintaining the appearance of propriety in behavior so as not to give just cause for offense.' [513] However, sorcery was employed in interpersonal conflicts as well, meaning that chiefs had no exclusive control over it: 'Sorcery is a more direct if more secret form of aggression and is practically precluded from being applied within the lineage by the fact that the spells used are in each case the property of a given lineage. While it is not clear whether they would be considered effective, it is very doubtful that they would be used against others who knew them or could be expected to know them. Data on sorcery are difficult to obtain for no one admits to being a sorcerer. Those cases of afflictions (blindness, psychosis, and paralysis) which were definitely attributed to a given sorcerer always applied to someone on another island, angry because his wife had been taken away, his property trespassed upon, or the like. Although various people on Romonum were said to know certain techniques of sorcery and many people expressed a fear of being sorcerized, no one would say that a given person had actually sorcerized anyone. The detailed body of knowledge of procedures in sorcery which exists and the circumstantial accounts of how it was formerly used certainly suggest very strongly that in the past Trukese deliberately sorcerized other Trukese. Whether this is the case today, however, is an open question. In the past [Page 150] it was used by master magicians, both against rival magicians and against the laity, and was also used in the punishment of thieves; but then as now sorcery was primarily the result of one person’s animosity against another and as such acted as a sanction against acts which might antagonize another. Thus anxiety about sorcery is another factor weighing the balance in favor of conformist behavior.' [514]

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though chiefs were legitimated by the gods [515] and possessed supernatural powers [516], sources do not indicate that they were also thought to descend from gods.

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ present ♥ The following set of quotes suggest that some chiefs were believed to become powerful benevolent spirits after death. 'The fate of the human body after death is eternal destruction, according to Truk ideas. It is not so with the souls. Their fate is a different one. The body is scarcely dead before the battle between the two souls reaches its climax. The [unknown]unu[unknown]au hurls itself upon the [unknown]unuot in order to devour it. The natives try to prevent this in that when it is night they immediately light a [Page 22] light or maintain a fire. The [unknown]unu[unknown]au is afraid of the gleam of the light and stays quiet. It remains in the vicinity of the body even after the burial. The existence that it leads is a miserable one. It creeps about like an animal and searches for food. Refuse and dirt are its dishes. This [unknown]unu[unknown]au is called sope after its separation from the body and is greatly feared by the people, since it is supposed to lay violent hands even on human beings. It is said that the sope also enters houses. Cat tracks are pointed out on beams and on human bodies, which are supposed to come from them. Protection is sought by smearing the houses with the juice of bitter herbs and especially aromatic ones. Strangely enough these sope like a stench.' [517] 'Some souls get into Ledidi, some settle down on the islands of bliss. All these restless, wandering /23/ souls are unpopular, for they can easily become angry and do harm. In this condition they are called önu[unknown]au (evil spirit), önuot (devouring spirit), [Page 25] önurot (gloomy spirit), or even sope again. They say of a person who cannot control himself in anger, who does harm to people: “ A nauni emen önu[unknown]au, he has taken on an evil spirit. Most fortunate is the soul that immediately, still at the grave, finds a guide, a guardian spirit (önusor = good spirit). It is spared any superfluous roaming about. It goes into the sky at once with the help of its guide and, if it stands the test by the fauled, even Önulap. The true ones are this kind of önu. To distinguish them from the gods and nature spirits they are also called önuaramas (spirit person). They are worshiped and invoked the most, in ordinary life more than the celestial one. In general they are favorably disposed toward human beings. They have many secondary names which contribute to their characterization: önuot (good spirit), önusor (guardian spirit), önusodub (spirit which does not deceive. These önu play an important role in the life of the uanönu (spirit carriers) and the ton abab önu (spirit invoker).' [518] 'Many old people of Truk think that the souls are assigned to different places in the sky according to their eila[unknown], that is, their totemic kin-group. They call the places bis, like the places of the gods. The souls live there, eat, drink, smoke, sleep, dance, etc., just as on earth. In moral aspects too they are true human beings. The bis of the kin group Atau is called Ola and stands under the chief Pulopul. The bis of the kin group Witö has the name of Familon and stands under the chief Sapelipun.' [519] 'Many of these önuot have made themselves a great name through the miraculous grantings of prayers which are attributed to them, for example, Olap (great man), a former chief of the island of Uman.' [520]

♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred absent ♥ The following set of quotes suggest that some chiefs were believed to become powerful benevolent spirits after death, but there is no indication that it was an automatic process. 'The fate of the human body after death is eternal destruction, according to Truk ideas. It is not so with the souls. Their fate is a different one. [523] 'Many of these önuot have made themselves a great name through the miraculous grantings of prayers which are attributed to them, for example, Olap (great man), a former chief of the island of Uman.' [524]

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though chiefs were legitimated by the gods [525], possessed supernatural powers [526], and sometimes became benevolent spirits after death [527], sources do not indicate that they were also thought to be gods in life.

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ absent ♥ As a clan-based society, the Chuukese were stratified according to lineages, and in 'each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [528] Moreover, chiefs were thought to be connected to the sky world, its gods and its mana: 'A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [529] Accordingly, a number of taboos were attached to chiefs: 'Everyone who was not a consanguineal or affinal relative of the chief was heavily “taboo from setting himself above” him. This involved saying “ faajiro” as an expression of respect on meeting a chief. If further required that one crouch or crawl in the chief’s presence if he was seated, for it was forbidden to have one’s head on a higher plane than his. One avoided initiating social interaction with a chief, unless the need was urgent, in which case one made him a gift and then waited the chief’s pleasure. One could not persist in refusing a chief’s request without being guilty of namanam tekija (arrogant behavior), nor could one address him harshly or belligerently. For persons other than his relatives, a chief occupied the most heavily taboo status with respect to behavior of this kind. (The other categories of persons who shared this status with the chief were jiuag and pweny (navigators).) His consanguineal and affinal kinsmen, however, behaved toward him in accordance with the status positions which he occupied as their kinsman, at least as far as their being “taboo from above” him was concerned. Other formalities were also accorded a chief. When he walked along a path with a party of people, he went first, unless the path was difficult. In the latter event someone went ahead to clear the way. While crawling behavior and the expression “ faajiro” are no longer exhibited, a chief still walks first today and the people still feel themselves to be “taboo from above” him.' [530]

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ As a clan-based society, the Chuukese were stratified according to lineages, and in 'each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. ♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ suspected unknown ♥ 'These large differences in wealth and life-style are galling to many Trukese who hold egalitarianism as a fundamental ethic; the drunken behavior of many young men in the community is not unrelated to perceived class differences. Several of the wealthier individuals in Peniyesene keep.22 caliber rifles to deal with persons who might try to break into their homes at night.' [534]
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ inferred absent ♥ We have not found much relevant material on 'ethnicity'.
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ absent ♥ Though much can be found about gender relations among the Chuukese (e.g. in terms of division of labour and property rights), the following quotes relate exclusively to the intersection between gender and religion/ritual. Menstruation and pregnancy were subject to taboos: 'The woman who is menstruating or pregnant has something unclean, contemptible, about her. She is isolated from the others; no one who holds a “religious position” as ida[unknown] may enter the little dwelling, prepare her food, or eat anything intended for her. The prohibition ends when the condition does.' [535] According to Bollig, women were traditionally forbidden from entering men's houses: 'The udd is a meeting house. The public meetings ( kobu[unknown]) take place here. Also the men who have to abstain from their wives for some reason sleep in it together. In former times it was strictly forbidden for women to enter the men’s houses, which is why the expression oloudd, that is, to sleep in the udd, is synonymous with sexual abstinence.' [536] However, Goodenough claims that women were generally permitted to enter meeting houses: 'Its wuut was the place where the unmarried young men of a lineage slept. Its older men, and at times the husbands of its women, also slept there whenever they were observing important sexual taboos. A meeting house was the lounging place for men during the evening, where they visited and told stories. In it visitors from other islands and districts were received, entertained, and put up for the night.' [537] 'Under aboriginal conditions a lineage’s wuut was not necessarily located near its jimw. It frequently stood by the shore, where it served as a canoe house ( wutten waa) as well as a meeting house ( wutten mwiic). A meeting house was not in itself taboo to women, although normally they did not enter it if men other than their husbands were present. Both men and women, however, could be present together at such feasts and dances as did not exclude one sex and were held in the meeting house. During certain ceremonies, men kept away from the meeting house and it was used exclusively by women. The women of a lineage could hold meetings of their own in its wuut.' [538] Women were nevertheless excluded from some ceremonial activities due to gendered ritual taboos: 'A few dances are also connected with the worship of the spirits. These are the dugia (dance with kurukur, that is, staffs of orange wood) and the älimod, a dance which is performed in a sitting position. The former is a men’s dance; the latter may be performed by both sexes. During the entire dancing period the dancers are shut up in the udd. This is surrounded by a fence which no unbidden person, especially no woman, may approach. No indecent word occurs in these spirit dances, in contrast to the other dances. They say that the spirits do not like indecent things in connection with the men, a statement which does not correspond with their views on other occasions.' [539] 'The pue is handled in different ways. The most important and solemn pue is the girot. The soupue makes the girot in the udd in the /66/ presence of his pupils. Women are not allowed to watch. As a matter of fact no woman is allowed to make any kind of pue.' [540] 'After the apu[unknown]etou, the canoe, which is called faremai (= önumai) if it is a rowing canoe, is tested. It goes out on the sea for the kitir (rowing or sailing race). No woman is allowed to board the faremai, and no food may be put in it. The food must go with the other canoes. In the center of the new canoe sits the souatomai, who caresses the canoe and invokes the spirits. Having reached water favorable for navigation, the crew starts to row, and the vessels shoot across the broad surface with the speed of arrows. The invocations of the suatomai become more and more animated; the iéfot, iéfot of the rowers, wilder and wilder. Strange /74/ canoes passing by must be careful about coming too close to the faremai, for it is sacred.' [541] Men in turn were excluded from some female-gendered activities, such as childbirth: 'It is not so simple for a child to be born on Truk. One can call it a near miracle if a child escapes all who struggle for its life before and after birth. The woman in childbirth is separated from the other occupants of the house. She often occupies a little house built especially for the birth. One or another older woman from among the relatives, called soufam or souetikar (midwives), is to be found with her. Entrance is forbidden to all men.' [542]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ inferred absent ♥ We have not found much relevant material on differentiation on the basis of location.

As a matter of fact no woman is allowed to make any kind of pue.' [551] 'After the apu[unknown]etou, the canoe, which is called faremai (= önumai) if it is a rowing canoe, is tested. It goes out on the sea for the kitir (rowing or sailing race). No woman is allowed to board the faremai, and no food may be put in it. The food must go with the other canoes. In the center of the new canoe sits the souatomai, who caresses the canoe and invokes the spirits. Having reached water favorable for navigation, the crew starts to row, and the vessels shoot across the broad surface with the speed of arrows. The invocations of the suatomai become more and more animated; the iéfot, iéfot of the rowers, wilder and wilder. Strange /74/ canoes passing by must be careful about coming too close to the faremai, for it is sacred.' [552] Men in turn were excluded from some female-gendered activities, such as childbirth: 'It is not so simple for a child to be born on Truk. One can call it a near miracle if a child escapes all who struggle for its life before and after birth. The woman in childbirth is separated from the other occupants of the house. She often occupies a little house built especially for the birth. One or another older woman from among the relatives, called soufam or souetikar (midwives), is to be found with her. Entrance is forbidden to all men.' [553]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes people from different locations ♣ inferred absent ♥ We have not found much relevant material on differentiation on the basis of location.

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present ♥ As a clan-based society, the Chuukese were stratified according to lineages, and in 'each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [554] Moreover, chiefs were thought to be connected to the sky world, its gods and its mana: 'A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [555] Accordingly, a number of taboos were attached to chiefs: 'Everyone who was not a consanguineal or affinal relative of the chief was heavily “taboo from setting himself above” him. This involved saying “ faajiro” as an expression of respect on meeting a chief. If further required that one crouch or crawl in the chief’s presence if he was seated, for it was forbidden to have one’s head on a higher plane than his. One avoided initiating social interaction with a chief, unless the need was urgent, in which case one made him a gift and then waited the chief’s pleasure. One could not persist in refusing a chief’s request without being guilty of namanam tekija (arrogant behavior), nor could one address him harshly or belligerently. For persons other than his relatives, a chief occupied the most heavily taboo status with respect to behavior of this kind. (The other categories of persons who shared this status with the chief were jiuag and pweny (navigators).) His consanguineal and affinal kinsmen, however, behaved toward him in accordance with the status positions which he occupied as their kinsman, at least as far as their being “taboo from above” him was concerned. Other formalities were also accorded a chief. When he walked along a path with a party of people, he went first, unless the path was difficult. In the latter event someone went ahead to clear the way. While crawling behavior and the expression “ faajiro” are no longer exhibited, a chief still walks first today and the people still feel themselves to be “taboo from above” him.' [556]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to Bollig, polygyny was fairly common: 'Polygamy (1) 1 a akaruu, he has two wives; a akaulu[unknown]at, he has three wives, etc. is very widespread on Truk. Men with two to nine wives are numerous. One becomes polygamous through the death of his brother, for out of brotherly love he has an obligation to take the widow for himself (levirate marriage). Another one marries a widow with daughters and takes the latter, as soon as they are old enough, as secondary wives. A third takes the sisters of his wife for himself, which is the case for most polygamous situations. The reasons for polygamy vary. One wants to keep landed property together by this means, or to concentrate it in his hands; another wants to have workers, since the secondary wives fish for him. Strangely enough, the motive forces for polygamy are said to be mostly the women themselves. They offer their daughters, sisters, [Page 109] and nieces to their husbands as secondary wives, in order that no strange family come in. This happens especially if they have no children, for if a strange woman comes in and gives birth to children, they will be pushed aside. They try to prevent this by means of the above measure. If polygamy has come about in this way, a peaceful life together is possible, for the women stay together in their own interests. But if the women come from different families or tribes, living together is out of the question. They live apart, and the man has the pleasant task of going back and forth. Unpleasantness and jealousy between the rivals are the order of the day. First one and then the other thinks she is being slighted. First one is tired, then the other. And the husband must always be on the move in order to keep everybody happy. Some polygamous situations which I knew were changing constantly. At one time the husband had four, then some ran away, later they increased to six, etc. A somewhat decent family life is inconceivable. The eventual /101/ children always go with the mother, but the father knows the means for drawing them to him when they are older, so that he will have a work force. Some polygamists are very generous with their wives. They are quickly ready to hand them over to their unmarried relatives, because it gives them advantages. They are able to take a new one and still consider the old one indirectly as their own.' [557] Polyandry, on the other hand, was rare: 'Polyandry seems to be rarer. I knew of only one case, in which two brothers had a common wife.' [558] But Caughey claims that monogamy was the norm: 'In the traditional society polygynous marriages rarely occurred. Since marriage sets up heavy obligations to the wife’s lineage, most men did not want to increase this burden, especially by marrying into different lineages. Interpreted differently, the same reasons provided the rationale for those few cases of polygny that did occur. A man who married more than one wife demonstrated his importance and power. It showed that he was capable of engaging in burdensome social relationships with more than one lineage or that he was able to turn this subordinate relationship into one where he dominated. A famous man of bravery on Fáánakkar was once married at the same time to five wives from four different lineages. Later, in a grand gesture of kindness, he gave one of them away to a wifeless brother.' [559]

♠ Ideology reinforces prosociality ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ charity ♣ absent ♥ Philanthropic foundations were introduced in the colonial period. Prior to colonization, informal patterns of self-help, neighbourly assistance and support from the matrilineage fulfilled these functions: 'Within its district each lineage owns a particular plot of land which is regarded as its “true home” or “lineage center” ( imweyiche). The dwelling houses of resident members are typically clustered at this site, and members living elsewhere frequently return here to visit. Persons residing at the lineage center will include some genealogical members of the lineage plus other people who have current links to resident members. Typically some of these will be persons of the same district while others will be members of other districts and occasionally of other islands.' [560] 'More than one lineage is important to an individual on aanakkar. He automatically has pressing obligations to serve others in his own lineages, and he has strong rights to demand support from them in return. He also has lesser but important rights and obligations to members of his father’s lineage and to members of his spouse’s lineage (for a man this will also be the lineage of his children). Finally he has minor obligations to his father’s father’s lineage and to his spouse’s father’s lineage.' [561] The variable may thus not be applicable to the pre-colonial Chuuk.
♠ production of public goods ♣ inferred absent ♥ It appears that there was little production of public works other than feasts prior to colonization (see below). Details on the construction of men's houses and the extent of cooperation involved therein are still needed. Even in the case of feasting, it is notable that the feast's hosts did not produce the food on offer, but rather received it from his guests and then re-distributed it among them [562].
♠ communal dining ♣ present ♥ Islanders prepared food for feasts held by chiefs: 'As noted above, the relations between a chief and the people of his district are patterned largely after the relations between the parties in divided ownership. As provisional title holders to territory, the various lineages owe the chief periodic gifts of produce from the land. These gifts are rendered at stated times in connection with feasts given in the chief’s honor. He fixes the time for these feasts in accordance with a seasonal calendar: two feasts during the breadfruit season, and two more during the turmeric season. The men of the various lineages prepare breadfruit in giant wooden bowls while the women fish. Other types of food are also collected. On the day of the feast each lineage brings its food in a procession, the men carrying the giant bowls on frames and chanting as they walk. The food is carried into the meeting house of the chief’s lineage. The chief then selects the largest bowl for himself and his lineage mates and redistributes the rest of the food to the assembled population. The distribution is made according to lineage and is roughly proportional to the size of each lineage. The contributions which each lineage makes are also proportional to the size of its membership and the amount of productive land which it controls. If a lineage fails in this obligation, the chief has the right to confiscate its holdings. Should he indicate dissatisfaction with the contribution of a particular lineage, its members will hasten to prepare additional food and other gifts to mollify him.' [563] Bollig describes the preparations for a festival: 'Both develop a feverish activity. The men prepare whole mountains of food. Whatever is found in the plantings is dragged out. The women too get no rest. They are constantly squatting in the water in order to snatch at a little tail. If the supplies of their own tribe are not sufficient, friends on other islands have [Page 201] to help out, for which equal help is given to them in a similar situation. There is movement on all roads on the morning of the fadanap day, for no Kanaka wants to be absent when there is something to chew. All are loaded with bananas, pineapples, sugar cane, coconuts, and fish, which they carry to the chief’s house, where everything is piled up. Very rich ones also bring such European goods as rice, biscuits, salmon; indeed, even beer and liquor could not be lacking in Japanese times. The most festive moment is the bringing of the large madün bowls. Cautiously they come rocking along, carried on the shoulders of strong men. The carriers are painted, /183/ adorned, and sing their emoir. When the fadanap participants are in two parties, they approach from different sides, singing to each other. But usually one tribe or the other takes it badly, since ancient hostility prevails among all the tribes. They quarrel with each other, throw food at their heads, and not infrequently bring knives and spears into play.' [564] Hospitality was expected, but food sorcery was designed to discourage the abuse of hospitality obligations: 'General food sorcery can be used to aggress against and attempt to control the actions of people who take too great advantage of hospitality patterns. These patterns require that all persons who are present any time food is consumed be invited to participate in the meal. The only strong sanction that can be used against those who become more or less permanent guests is the threat of food sorcery. In addition, these sanctions can be manipulated to keep relatives eating at home, in which case they can also be induced to contribute their labor to help in supplying provisions.' [565] 'The food sorcery of sub-division B placed a restraint on those who would take undue advantage of hospitality patterns. It can work two ways, by helping prevent depredations on a lineage’s own food supply, and sometimes through illness, reminding lineage members that their safest place is back home helping their own lineages.' [566]
♠ morality ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Morality is somewhat hard to code for in the absence of written records. See below for more information.

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ 3 ♥ The following appears to be the only reference to human sacrifice among the Chuukese, in the sense of the act of killing a human being in order to please or placate a supernatural being: 'The spirit Paladiu on the island of Falanu is even more modest. Human excrement satisfies him as an offering. During a war human sacrifices seem to have been offered also. At least cases are known where captives had their throats cut over the graves of those who had fallen, so that the blood would be sprinkled over them.' [567]

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ inferred present ♥ The following appears to be the only reference to human sacrifice among the Chuukese, in the sense of the act of killing a human being in order to please or placate a supernatural being: 'The spirit Paladiu on the island of Falanu is even more modest. Human excrement satisfies him as an offering. During a war human sacrifices seem to have been offered also. At least cases are known where captives had their throats cut over the graves of those who had fallen, so that the blood would be sprinkled over them.' [568]

At least cases are known where captives had their throats cut over the graves of those who had fallen, so that the blood would be sprinkled over them.' [569]

Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic ♥ 'Besides Önulap, the Jupiter of the Truk people, there is still a large number of others, in part male, in part female, in part sympathetic, in part unsympathetic. The gods in their customs are often not a bit better than the Truk people themselves. They eat, drink, dance, play, marry, and also perform many a trick which is not at all appropriate for gods. Even Önulap, although the best one in the sky, is a true old Kanaka chief, who lies on his mat in his hut and stares straight ahead. Just like the Truk regions, they have also transplanted in the sky the inhabitants with their good and bad qualities. Is one justified in ridiculing or blaming them, since we have observed the same thing among anciently civilized peoples. Let us look more closely at some of these gods. Semenkoror (father of wisdom) is a brother of Önulap. Human beings are in debt to him for many skills, for which he stands in the highest esteem among all the natives. Silelap (the master), Äremei (achiever), Ämarourou (chatterer), Nugeila[unknown] (the mediator in the sky), Soukuli[unknown], Ororofit, Semoutut (the one not moving). Nothing was to be learned about the activities of these gods individually.'[577] 'The most unsympathetic one of all is Olfäd (the treader). He was given the name “Treader” because, according to a legend, he is supposed to have produced a great number of small islands near Uman by a step with his foot. That is, a girl whom he wanted to marry reproached him for his poverty, whereupon in anger he trod [Page 8] these islands out of the sea. The most extraordinary things are told about Olfäd. On account of his cunning, his evil tricks, he is unpopular among gods and human beings.'[578] 'Besides the gods, there is also a large number of goddesses, among whom the two sisters Nisärere and Nipälou are the most interesting. They are regarded as patronesses of the art of weaving and are frequently invoked by the women.'[579] 'Neanean (the wisher) is a happy goddess. She need only wish for something and it is there immediately.'[580] 'What Olfäd is among the gods, Inemes (mother of the face) is among the goddesses. Her love potions and love magic confuse all hearts. She is the protectress of lechery, the Venus of the Truk people. Opinions vary concerning her appearance. Some call her ugly, infected with a skin disease; others, as pretty. The latter seems to be the correct one.'[581] 'Inemes also has a daughter, who has the significant name of Ururupuin (night flirt). With regard to morals she has the same point of view as her mother.'[582] 'The Truk people think of the sky as inhabited by gods, and the earth, by spirits. Yet all these spirits come from Önulap and are subject to his leadership.'[583] 'Nigoubub (the procreatress) is a female spirit. She produced earth and human beings at Önulap’s command. The islanders conceive of Nigoubub as a female eel at whose breast the earth rests. When she turns, the earth trembles. That is the Truk people’s explanation for earthquakes. Women who desire children or those who are in labor worship Nigoubub. Nigoubub’s companion is Niro[unknown]epü[unknown] (she who hears the sound). She lives in holes in the earth in the shape of a large crab. She comes out at night and creeps about. The sound of the steps of approaching human beings drives her away. If she bites a person in the foot, he will become deaf. In the säd (3) 3 säd is low water along the land. live the tölöken (4) 4 tol is peak, comb in the case of chickens, dorsal fin in the case of fish. ken = sharp, pointed. [illegible]. They are the creators and rulers of the säd and of the fish living in these quarters. Loubon (he who lives in the interior) is an important tölöken. Weather and typhoons are subject to him.'[584] 'Creators and rulers of the deep waters are the osen madau (voices of the sea) and the nidudun (female sea-divers). The former are male, the latter female. They are often mentioned in the rales of the seafarers.'[585] 'On the mountains dwell the notorious osen tuk (voices of the mountains), who have already devoured many a human being. The one called Önun pelien or Resim (5) 5 resim = rainbow. is regarded as the ruler of the rainbow and therefore especially dangerous for seafarers. The rainbow does not evoke any friendly feelings among the islanders, since they consider it a sign of death. Among the women Soumöröges is loved and hated, for the children’s souls are subject to him. He brings them and takes them away again upon the death of the child. Kir is a malicious spirit who dries up the breasts of nursing women.'[586] 'Spirits, the so-called önunumai (3) 3 önu = spirit, mai = breadfruit. , also dwell in the trees, [Page 14] especially in the breadfruit trees. Säli[unknown]eruu (the two-eared) is a spirit who sows discontent among human beings. Ke de Säli[unknown]eruu, you should not be two-eared, is an admonition frequently given to those who let themselves be influenced by all sides.'[587] 'Spirits, the önunupun, are likewise found in the earth. All these spirits, whose number is boundless, are more or less forces of nature, phenomena which the natives are unable to explain and therefore ascribe to spirits. The natives imagine these nature spirits in every possible shape. Some are hardly distinguishable from true human beings. Others have long hair all over their bodies and the teeth of a boar. Some have eyes in front and in back, etc. Many islanders [unknown]bstinately maintain that they have seen such beings. They play an important part in the old legends [unknown]and tales. The story of brave men who defeated and rendered harmless such man-eating spirits always excites general applause among the audience. According to the view of the Truk people everything has a soul, even plants and animals. But their souls perish with the matter. The /14/ human being has two souls, a [unknown]unuot and a [unknown]unu[unknown]au, that is, a good one and a bad one, or, better said, a beautiful soul and an ugly soul. The beautiful soul resembles a beautiful person; the ugly soul, an ugly person. The manner of existence of these two souls is different. The [unknown]unu[unknown]au has grown together with the body and cannot leave it during its lifetime. [Page 15] The [unknown]unuot, on the other hand, rests on the shoulder of the person. It is lightly winged, like a bird, and can fly away. But an absence of some time would result in the early death of the person. The natives maintain about insane persons that they no longer have any [unknown]unuot. The Truk people explain dreams by the flying away of the good soul. On Truk “A dreams of B” means nothing but that the souls of A and B have flown away and have met in the gathering of the souls of the deceased. If one frightens an islander so that he starts, he will say: “ A äsilo [unknown]uni,” that is, “my soul has flown away.” When a dying person has ceased struggling, it is said: “ A lo [unknown]unun, his soul has gone away.”'[588]
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ We define "omnipresent supernatural beings" as "all-seeing supernatural beings who are present everywhere and can observe all actions, but can't necessarily read minds or know the intentions underlying actions". The following quote suggests that Önulap was omnipresent in this sense. 'Önulap is the highest of the gods. He is the father of the gods and human beings. Everything comes from him, including illness and misfortune. [...] Önulap also knows everything that human beings do, since a turn of speech runs: “ Si a fadal le mesen Önulap” (we walk in the presence of the great spirit).'[589]
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ We define "omniscient supernatural beings" as "all-knowing, mind-reading supernatural beings who have access to all socially strategic information." The following quote suggests that Önulap knows all that humans being do, but not necessarily what they think. 'Önulap is the highest of the gods. He is the father of the gods and human beings. Everything comes from him, including illness and misfortune. [...] Önulap also knows everything that human beings do, since a turn of speech runs: “ Si a fadal le mesen Önulap” (we walk in the presence of the great spirit).'[590]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ inferred present ♥ 'Önulap is the highest of the gods. He is the father of the gods and human beings. Everything comes from him, including illness and misfortune. Everything is tinien Önulap (fate, messenger of the great spirit). Human beings too belong to Önulap. The Truk people believe that Whites are the first-born, the Blacks, the Kanakas, the younger children of the great spirit. They express this in the following phrase: “ Naun Önulap muänidi re uon, naun /7/ sodubudur kanaka.” Önulap also knows everything that human beings do, since a turn of speech runs: “ Si a fadal le mesen Önulap” (we walk in the presence of the great spirit). The gods and spirits are also subject to Önulap. Whatever they do they do only at his command. Since Önulap stands so high and also does not come down to the uanönu like most of the other gods, his individual worship is rarer. In important matters, for example, in case of war, he is invoked.'[591] It's not very clear whether Önulap himself punished or rewarded mortals, but it is clear that the gods in general actively intervened in human affairs--though possibly not on the basis of moral principles. 'The natives express the relationship of the önu to the human being and vice versa in the following parallelism: Emen a önubodede, a feiot; emen a önumamau a [unknown]au, “when one is obedient to the önu, then he is happy; but when he is rebellious toward the önu, then he is unhappy.” In other words: For one who obeys the spirits, it will go well; for one who disobeys them, it will go badly. Having everything go well is brought about by the protection of the önu; things go badly because of their inflicting punishment on human beings. Thus, everything adverse in the world is punishment for the violation of the commands or prohibitions which the önu, and especially Önulap, have imposed on h human beings. The Truk people also say about disagreeable events: tinien Önulap, that is, decree of the “Great Spirit.” When an önu punishes a human being, the natives express this in the phrase önu a ku, the spirit has bitten. This “bite” manifests itself in various ways: by the swelling of limbs, stiff neck, maimings, epilepsy, sudden death, etc. The “bites” are regularly directed [Page 29] against a person’s life, which accounts for the fact that the natives are concerned about manau, life, more than anything else. But by life they almost exclusively mean manauen inisit, life of the body, inasmuch as, despite their knowledge of the other world, they have no desire whatsoever to go to the other world.'[592]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ active ♥ There is clearly a High God, Önulap. 'Önulap is the highest of the gods. He is the father of the gods and human beings. Everything comes from him, including illness and misfortune. Everything is tinien Önulap (fate, messenger of the great spirit). Human beings too belong to Önulap. The Truk people believe that Whites are the first-born, the Blacks, the Kanakas, the younger children of the great spirit. They express this in the following phrase: “ Naun Önulap muänidi re uon, naun /7/ sodubudur kanaka.” Önulap also knows everything that human beings do, since a turn of speech runs: “ Si a fadal le mesen Önulap” (we walk in the presence of the great spirit). The gods and spirits are also subject to Önulap. Whatever they do they do only at his command. Since Önulap stands so high and also does not come down to the uanönu like most of the other gods, his individual worship is rarer. In important matters, for example, in case of war, he is invoked.'[593] It's not very clear whether Önulap himself punished or rewarded mortals, but it is clear that the gods in general actively intervened in human affairs--though possibly not on the basis of moral principles. 'The natives express the relationship of the önu to the human being and vice versa in the following parallelism: Emen a önubodede, a feiot; emen a önumamau a [unknown]au, “when one is obedient to the önu, then he is happy; but when he is rebellious toward the önu, then he is unhappy.” In other words: For one who obeys the spirits, it will go well; for one who disobeys them, it will go badly. Having everything go well is brought about by the protection of the önu; things go badly because of their inflicting punishment on human beings. Thus, everything adverse in the world is punishment for the violation of the commands or prohibitions which the önu, and especially Önulap, have imposed on h human beings. The Truk people also say about disagreeable events: tinien Önulap, that is, decree of the “Great Spirit.” When an önu punishes a human being, the natives express this in the phrase önu a ku, the spirit has bitten. This “bite” manifests itself in various ways: by the swelling of limbs, stiff neck, maimings, epilepsy, sudden death, etc. The “bites” are regularly directed [Page 29] against a person’s life, which accounts for the fact that the natives are concerned about manau, life, more than anything else. But by life they almost exclusively mean manauen inisit, life of the body, inasmuch as, despite their knowledge of the other world, they have no desire whatsoever to go to the other world.'[594]

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ inferred absent ♥ Based on early 20th century accounts: "The attractive traits, associated with one's mirror image and comprising one's good soul, joined the gods in the sky world, while the unattractive traits, associated with one's shadow and comprising one's bad soul, became a potentially dangerous ghost that did not leave the region of substantive things. ... they pertained to one's place in the cosmos and had little, if anything, to do with a person's psyche (tiip) or with what kind of person one was while living" [595]. "The fate of one's good soul after death was not a matter of reward or punishment for good or evil deeds. Its fate in the spirit world depended more on its psychic qualities, as such, than on specific acts." [596] People psyches (irresponsible, wise etc.) could determine what happened to their good soul, but this was predetermined and had nothing to do with their actions in life [597] However, there is an example of someone using a spirit to kill but it seems a human use of an evil spirit rather than any kind of deterrent to kill an enemy 'The incident begins just after assassins, who sneaked up to his meeting house and poked their rifles through the thatch wall, have shot and killed the chief Mwaatejinyk: “All of the occupants of the wuut fled in panic except for Wenejog... Mwaatejinyk’s grandson. He picked up his grandfather’s body and carried it out of the wuut to prevent its falling into the assassin’s hands. Carrying it down to the north shore of the island, he proceeded west along the rocks on the shore and then climbed back up to... where Mwaatejinyk had lived.... Here Wenejóg buried his grandfather in an upright position leaving his head above ground and facing in the direction of the Pwereka 1 house (his enemy’s lineage center).... He then propped Mwaatejinyk’s mouth open with a stick so that his ghost would go and devour the Mwej people in revenge. This deed earned for Wenejóg a great reputation for bravery” (Goodenough 1951:155-156).'[598]
♠ supernatural enforcement of fairness ♣ inferred present ♥ Based on early 20th century accounts: Sky God "'Good Determining' (Oororofich), said to be a brother of 'Father of Determining', was another resident of 'Under Brow'. Concerned with the maintenance of good relations among people, he was angered by theft and other antisocial behaviour." [599]. Although not from Chuuk, the same rainbow spirit in the Mortock Islands has been recorded as becoming angry on human conduct at sea (for example taking food reserved for the navigator or other crimes) and requires appeasement when appearing. [600]
♠ supernatural enforcement of human reciprocity ♣ inferred present ♥ This is 20th century ethnographic data but has continuity from late 19th century practices: Gods had control of a spirit power that could influence how well other's lives went in terms of work and property. An enuusor was a kind of spirit that could dish out power to mediums with knowledge of sorcery and spells. If one was "effective" in building houses, fulfilling family and social duties etc. they had a better chance of becoming a spirit who could influence the lives of their kin from the afterlife: "A person who appeared to be well above the average in effectiveness might be seen as having a good soul with a better than average chance of becoming an enuusor (effecting spirit)" [601]. Based on early 20th century accounts: "When a dead person's soul that was "unworthy" (Bollig's word, presumable for having an inappropriate psychic disposition) approached 'Great Spirit's' house, the two stones clapped together and "killed" it." [602]
♠ supernatural enforcement of property rights ♣ inferred present ♥ Based on early 20th century accounts: Sky God "'Good Determining' (Oororofich), said to be a brother of 'Father of Determining', was another resident of 'Under Brow'. Concerned with the maintenance of good relations among people, he was angered by theft and other antisocial behaviour." [603]. "A good soul might be angered by failure to pass on to its surviving children a share of the property it held while alive. There was a saying that ... 'one who has been polite to spirits fares well; one who has been disrespectful of spirts fares badly'. Against such illness no medicine was needed. Cure resulted from corrective action taken in regard to the offended spirit. ... local spirits caused illness if one trespassed on their locality." [604]
♠ supernatural enforcement of kinship obligations ♣ inferred present ♥ "Quarrels among siblings were most regrettable and could, if serious, bring illness to themselves or their children through the displeasure of their deceased elders." [605] This is 20th century ethnographic data but has continuity from late 19th century practices: "There was much talk also of demons and ghosts; and adults made no effort to conceal their own fear of them from their children. Illness was attributed to the malevolence of demons and ghosts. A person was vulnerable to attack by them when he was away from human settlement and was alone. This is frequent reference in Gladwin's data to parents warning children against going off by themselves (Gladwin and Sarason 1953). Good spirits of the dead, whose favor could be helpful, would also punish their living relatives with sickness and death for improper behaviour and ill will among people who were close kin." [606] Based on early 20th century accounts: Spirits of the soil could give elephantiasis through biting those who remained on their taro patches too late into the day. There were similar sea and mountain spirits. Also dangerous tree and breadfruit spirits, which required the appropriate propitiatory singing before cutting them down. [607] Many dangerous spirits who preyed on pregnant women who went outdoors, eating their foetus and causing miscarriages. Also 'Grabbers' who caused illness and distress to infants who went out late afternoon with their mothers. [608] "In any case, surviving close kin did not want to have the good soul of the deceased angry with them for their neglect. Its anger could lead it to inflict illness and possible death on one or more of them. The mourning and bringing of gifts that characterized the proceedings immediately following death were also intended to make the deceased's good soul well disposed to surviving kin." [609]
♠ supernatural enforcement of in-group loyalty ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Based on early 20th century accounts: "A medium could also pass on a warning from the spirit that through their conduct people were likely to incur its wrath and endanger their community." [610] however, there does not seem to be any examples of supernatural enforcement of loyalty in terms of punishment for a lack of loyalty or support, so punishing a community for one's actions is not enough to code present.
♠ supernatural enforcement of respect ♣ inferred present ♥ This is 20th century ethnographic data but has continuity from late 19th century practices: Political priests called Itang wielded far more spiritual power (Manaman) than others. [611] Based on early 20th century accounts: "Miracles were a product of manaman. Ordinary people lacked it. They could obtain access to it by learning the spells that activated recipes and medicines and that offered success in war and other undertakings. Specialists had access to it through the rituals associated with their specialties. Its ultimate source was the spirit world, especially 'Under Brow', it was obtained by humans from 'human god' ancestors and from 'effecting spirits' of the dead through their mediums." [612]. "It seems reasonable to infer, therefore, that beliefs about the fate of the soul provided at least some incentive for people to endeavor to be correct in their behaviour to others and, especially, to be obedient to the authority of chiefs, lineage heads, and older siblings of same sex." [613]
♠ supernatural enforcement of bravery ♣ inferred present ♥ Based on early 20th century accounts: Bravery was an attribute males needed to show, perhaps like the nearby Namoluk the good souls who died in battle, and were buried at sea, went to the god of war 'Rainbow' (Resiim). [614]. In addition, as described above, those who were "effective" (perhaps brave in war) and had a good "psyche" (which included bravery for men) had a higher chance of becoming spirits who could influence the lives of their kin in the afterlife.
♠ supernatural enforcement of purity ♣ inferred present ♥ Based on early 20th century accounts: Sharp Water Spirits could make people ill or "eaten" or "bitten" by the sharp water if they did not follow certain rules, such as playing or swimming too much in the same waters, not abstaining from sexual intercourse or not abstaining from fish food before visiting the reefs where the spirits dwelled. [615]. To become a medium one needed to observe taboos on food and abstain from sexual activity to be chosen by a spirit. Spirits can also terminate relationships with mediums if one does not observe the taboos. [616]. "During the period of this daily dance, the men who took part in it had to sleep in the meeting house, avoiding having sex. Violation of this restriction would anger 'Lord of South' and result in no breadfruit that year." [617]. Heron sickness for those who ate certain foods under sacred trees. [618] Based on early 20th century accounts
♠ general supernatural enforcement of morality ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ other supernatural enforcement ♣ inferred present ♥ Based on early 20th century accounts: On the Great Spirit "Everything came from him, including sickness and misfortune. ... Consistent with this are the reports from surrounding atolls, but not from the Chuuk itself, that 'Great Spirit' held in his hands the life threads of living persons. These threads were connected to the tops of people's heads. If 'Great Spirit' disapproved of a person's behaviour, he or his mother tore the thread, and the person died (Damm and Sarfert 1935:192; Kramer 1937:150-151, 279) [619]. Based on early 20th century accounts of nearby Namoluk, even if not Chuuk Islands it may be relevant: "It is reported from Namoluk that there the souls of people who had "sinned" in life were not admitted to the sky world but were taken by 'Stamper' to a "place of damnation" called Leenchiichi (sic), chiichi being the name of a tree whose sap causes burning irritation to one's skin. There they were cast into a great hole, called 'Sea of Muck', where they remained unable to move. (Girschner 1912:192)" [620]

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ inferred absent ♥ Caughey claims that belligerence was generally valued in men prior to colonization: 'However, on Romónum it appears that neither the exclusive stress on respectfulness, nor the “prohibition of aggression” (Swartz 1965:37), nor the unaggressive style of social conduct were characteristic prior to the German pacification of Truk in 1903. In the 19th century, the people of Truk as a whole were considered highly aggressive in comparison to those of other areas in Micronesia. “As is well known, 19th century whalers and other travelers avoided Truk because of its reputation for belligerency toward outsiders, and within the atoll itself warfare was frequent. Although the Spanish held title to Truk from 1885 to 1889 and theoretically administered it [Page 174] from their base at Ponape, they actually did not govern the island. The bellicose nature of the people of Truk was a factor in the Spanish avoidance of the area” (Swartz 1965:17). My information about warfare on 19th century Faanakkar clearly fits this picture as do the reports of Germans who lived on Truk in the early 20th century. “Truk was one big battlefield. Island fought against island, tribe against tribe. On one day two villages were co-operating; on the next day they were fighting each other. There was constant killing in an ever changing situation” (Bollig 1927:104). Evidently aggressiveness was also common on 19th century Romonum. In tracing the history of Romonum from 1850 to 1903, Goodenough notes that the history begins with a fight, and his account shows that it was permeated by assassinations, bloody feuds, sorcery battles, and warfare with neighboring islands (1951:148-161). Gladwin and Sarason (1953:41) provide additional supporting evidence. It also appears that this aggressive behavior was associated with an ideal of bravery similar to that on Faanakkar. An example from Goodenough’s history of Romonum will illustrate this point. The incident begins just after assassins, who sneaked up to his meeting house and poked their rifles through the thatch wall, have shot and killed the chief Mwaatejinyk: “All of the occupants of the wuut fled in panic except for Wenejog... Mwaatejinyk’s grandson. He picked up his grandfather’s body and carried it out of the wuut to prevent its falling into the assassin’s hands. Carrying it down to the north shore of the island, he proceeded west along the rocks on the shore and then climbed back up to... where Mwaatejinyk had lived.... Here Wenejóg buried his grandfather in an upright position leaving his head above ground and facing in the direction of the Pwereka 1 house (his enemy’s lineage center).... He then propped Mwaatejinyk’s mouth open with a stick so that his ghost would go and devour the Mwej people in revenge. This deed earned for Wenejóg a great reputation for bravery” (Goodenough 1951:155-156).'[621] Children were taught to be self-reliant and wary of the intentions of others: 'Small children were much held, fed on demand, and never left alone. They slept on the same mat with their mothers. By age 3 they were expected to begin to look after themselves. Children were lectured on correct social behavior, but were not held fully accountable for it until they became junior adults. Parents used switches to punish. Often persuaded to do what others wanted with promises that were broken afterwards, children learned to be wary of the intentions of others. They enjoyed much freedom to play, sharpening their physical skills. Transmission of special lore and knowledge became increasingly important as children grew to be young adults. The eldest son and eldest daughter were treated differently from other children. Their persons were inviolate, their wishes were honored, and they were not liable to physical punishment.'[622]
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ inferred present ♥ 'More than one lineage is important to an individual on aanakkar. He automatically has pressing obligations to serve others in his own lineages, and he has strong rights to demand support from them in return. He also has lesser but important rights and obligations to members of his father’s lineage and to members of his spouse’s lineage (for a man this will also be the lineage of his children). Finally he has minor obligations to his father’s father’s lineage and to his spouse’s father’s lineage.'[623]
♠ respect for authority ♣ inferred present ♥ Chiefs were owed food prestations for feasts: 'As noted above, the relations between a chief and the people of his district are patterned largely after the relations between the parties in divided ownership. As provisional title holders to territory, the various lineages owe the chief periodic gifts of produce from the land. These gifts are rendered at stated times in connection with feasts given in the chief’s honor. He fixes the time for these feasts in accordance with a seasonal calendar: two feasts during the breadfruit season, and two more during the turmeric season. The men of the various lineages prepare breadfruit in giant wooden bowls while the women fish. Other types of food are also collected. On the day of the feast each lineage brings its food in a procession, the men carrying the giant bowls on frames and chanting as they walk. The food is carried into the meeting house of the chief’s lineage. The chief then selects the largest bowl for himself and his lineage mates and redistributes the rest of the food to the assembled population. The distribution is made according to lineage and is roughly proportional to the size of each lineage. The contributions which each lineage makes are also proportional to the size of its membership and the amount of productive land which it controls. If a lineage fails in this obligation, the chief has the right to confiscate its holdings.
