Should he indicate dissatisfaction with the contribution of a particular lineage, its members will hasten to prepare additional food and other gifts to mollify him.'[624] Interactions between chiefs and commoners were regulated by a number of taboos: 'Everyone who was not a consanguineal or affinal relative of the chief was heavily “taboo from setting himself above” him. This involved saying “ faajiro” as an expression of respect on meeting a chief. If further required that one crouch or crawl in the chief’s presence if he was seated, for it was forbidden to have one’s head on a higher plane than his. One avoided initiating social interaction with a chief, unless the need was urgent, in which case one made him a gift and then waited the chief’s pleasure. One could not persist in refusing a chief’s request without being guilty of namanam tekija (arrogant behavior), nor could one address him harshly or belligerently. For persons other than his relatives, a chief occupied the most heavily taboo status with respect to behavior of this kind. (The other categories of persons who shared this status with the chief were jiuag and pwëny (navigators).) His consanguineal and affinal kinsmen, however, behaved toward him in accordance with the status positions which he occupied as their kinsman, at least as far as their being “taboo from above” him was concerned. Other formalities were also accorded a chief. When he walked along a path with a party of people, he went first, unless the path was difficult. In the latter event someone went ahead to clear the way. While crawling behavior and the expression “ faajiro” are no longer exhibited, a chief still walks first today and the people still feel themselves to be “taboo from above” him.'[625] 'Arrogance' was socially censured according to Caughey: 'These two styles of social interaction seem to be associated with a difference in character values. In writing about Romónum culture in the modern period, ethnographers never mention the ideal of strong thought, and although there are brief allusions to bravery it is not presented as a significant ideal. The literature on Romonum does refer, again and again, to the other major character value we found on Faanakkar, the importance of mosonoson, which is translated as “humility” or “respectfulness” as opposed to namanam tekiya, which is translated as “haughtiness” or “arrogant behavior”. Given these translations of the value labels and the examples furnished, it appears that the meanings of these terms are similar on Romonum and Faanakkar.'[626] 'It is expected that people will despise a person of arrogance and that there will be much hostile gossip about him. The resulting shame has driven a number of persons to suicide. Because they hate him, it is also assumed that people will eagerly seek a chance to do him in, and that he will become a target for “foul play” ( sorongngaw). This term refers to sorcery and other stealthy aggressive acts such as ambush and arson. Elders frequently warn younger kinsmen who are acting rudely to be more respectful, lest they meet an early death. Another [Page 51] person’s failure to offer even a single required word of respect is sometimes considered adequate justification for using sorcery against him. Conversely, respectfulness is universally praised. It is also assumed that other people will feel sympathy towards such a person and treat him, in turn, with kindly respectfulness.'[627] It is unclear whether his comments apply only to the colonial period or whether the pre-colonial period is included here as well. It appears that in the colonial period, there was considerable variation in moral values from island to island (see also below on 'bravery'). The relationship between 'arrogance' and bravery or belligerence is unclear for the colonial period.
♠ purity ♣ present ♥ Incest within the kin group was prohibited: 'There are not many obstacles to marriage on Truk. Under all conditions marriage within the same kin group is absolutely forbidden (totemism), even if the common root goes back ten degrees. For various kin groups marriage is not permitted, for relatives of the first degree, and, to be sure, in the direct line and collateral line. Therefore marriages between father and daughter, mother and son, and brother and sister are [Page 108] regarded as reprehensible. In this connection adopted siblings and half siblings are regarded as true siblings. It very often happens, however, that a man who marries a widow with an adult daughter takes the latter as a second wife. That is regarded as entirely proper. I have known of one single case in which a Truk chief married his own daughter and had several children with her. The natives frown upon such illegal marriages. They call them, rather crudely, pupulun pig (pig marriage).'[628] 'All the Truk people are divided into eila[unknown] (kin groups / Sippen/). The mother is bearer of the eila[unknown]. In any case this is connected with the doctrine of Nigoubub, who, as the origin of life, is also female. All children follow the eila[unknown] of the mother. Marriage within the same eila[unknown] is not permitted. A Witö woman may never marry a Witö man. Such a marriage is regarded as incest and is never permitted, even if the common ancestors of the loving couple lie many generations back. On the other hand, marriages between uncle and niece, male cousin and female cousin, father and stepdaughter are not rarities, as long as the eila[unknown] is different. Marriages within the same eila[unknown] were formerly punished by death. Even now this [Page 89] marriage is still detested, even among Christians. I was acquainted with a single case, and the common judgment was: “The man is bad, for he married his sister.” One inquires about the eila[unknown], for example, in the case of women, “ fin med en? (Woman, what sort are you?),” whereupon the answer comes, for for example, “ fin Witö.” For men, the question runs, “ muan meden? (Man, what sort are you?).”'[629] Notions of purity/impurity were associated with dirt, food and intercourse: '1. An unclean person is one who has stepped into sonepuin (1) 1 son = sediment, dregs; puin, night. The soiling can easily happen during nocturnal wanderings on the beach, which the Kanakas use as a toilet. The uncleanness lasts two, four, six, or eight days. During this time the one concerned must refrain from all religious acts. He himself can choose the number of days in proportion to his repentance and his zeal in the worship of the spirits. But it is expressly required that it must be an even number of days or nights, since the odd numbers are unfavorable.'[630] 'An unclean person is one who has already partaken of food. They express this defect with the words u a olokon, which corresponds somewhat to our “ ich bin nicht mehr nüchtern” /I am no longer fasting/.'[631] '3. An unclean person is one who has had sexual intercourse, no matter whether marital or extramarital, since the natives make no distinction in this. This defect is designated with the expression u se oloudd, that is literally, I have not slept in the udd (men’s house). Namely, one must consider the fact that no woman was allowed to enter the udd in olden times. Therefore the men who stayed there overnight could not have sexual intercourse. For that reason the expression “to sleep in the udd” means the same as to be continent. Standing in contrast to oloudd is olaim. It means to sleep in the im (dwelling), therefore, to have sexual intercourse.'[632] Accordingly, abstinence was required for the performance of some tasks: 'This oloudd is also a condition for other activities, as well as in connection with offerings, probably because they are subject to the influence of the spirits in a special way. It is required: If the men are obliged to be continent for one of the reasons mentioned above, this does not hold eo ipso for the women as well. They remain completely untouched. One can imagine what bad effects this practice, praiseworthy in itself, must have on family life during a long period. Only in the case of the weierek do the women on some islands also seem to be obligated to be continent. For example, if a man has bad luck because sharks spoil his fishing, he accuses his wife as though she were to blame for it on account of her incontinence. Other islands, on the other hand, deny the obligation of the women.'[633] Pregnancy, menstruation and childbirth were also associated with impurity: 'The woman who is menstruating or pregnant has something unclean, contemptible, about her. She is isolated from the others; no one who holds a “religious position” as ida[unknown] may enter the little dwelling, prepare her food, or eat anything intended for her. The prohibition ends when the condition does.'[634] 'As long as the child is small, the mother remains free of all work. She does not go fishing and in general does nothing other than merely sit with the child. But nobody will reproach her for it. Her husband brings her food, which is especially made for her ( mo[unknown]o ew fam), since she is still considered unclean. But he is not allowed to stay with her and eat with her as long as the child is not okotap, that is, turns on the mat independently.'[635] Violations of ritual taboos were punished by spirits: 'The people have a great fear of violating these binin. Anyone who violates a binin incurs the penalty of the revenge of spirit concerned, the one that issued it. He becomes feienau. This is the punishment that the people of Truk abhor. They have little understanding for moral defect. One who commits a bad deed and [Page 42] thereby becomes feienau has “sinned.” One who does not fall into this has not committed a sin. That in general is the principle of all Truk people. The conception of sin in the Christian sense is lacking among them. The term dibis that they have now was introduced from the outside and is identical with the above-mentioned feienau, according to the view of the natives.'[636]
♠ kinship ♣ present ♥ Matrilocal kin groups acted as cooperative units: 'The cooperative extended family is responsible for the provision of material wealth for its members. This family has in the past consisted of a group of own sisters, their spouses, their daughters, married and unmarried, and their unmarried sons and brothers, residing matrilocally. Today, the demise of the large family house has sometimes led to [Page 19] a grouping of sisters’ houses around a common family hearth. In other cases today, where it is more economically beneficial, married couples will reside patrilocally, while still others will set up nuclear family households. Families will often gain additional recruits from various sources such as teen-age children, dissatisfied with treatment in their own homes, who may be taken in as a pwipwi (sibling of the same sex) by some member of the family who happens to take a temporary or permanent liking to them, or among orphans and their spouses if they are still too young to set up households of their own at the time they are left without families. Cooperation for the benefit of the family extends outside this unit but within this unit it is more rigidly regulated. Other units formed for procuring biologic necessities are informal, transitory and voluntary except for those associated with the public welfare. In this last instance the chief generally assigns certain quantities of work to each family unit, who may in turn apportion it among their members as they see fit. This is done in the case of food for feasts, building of public works, etc.'[637] Males had obligations to their matrilineage as well as father's and wives' lineages: 'Within its district each lineage owns a particular plot of land which is regarded as its “true home” or “lineage center” ( imweyiche). The dwelling houses of resident members are typically clustered at this site, and members living elsewhere frequently return here to visit. Persons residing at the lineage center will include some genealogical members of the lineage plus other people who have current links to resident members. Typically some of these will be persons of the same district while others will be members of other districts and occasionally of other islands.'[638] 'More than one lineage is important to an individual on aanakkar. He automatically has pressing obligations to serve others in his own lineages, and he has strong rights to demand support from them in return. He also has lesser but important rights and obligations to members of his father’s lineage and to members of his spouse’s lineage (for a man this will also be the lineage of his children). Finally he has minor obligations to his father’s father’s lineage and to his spouse’s father’s lineage.'[639] Husbands and children retained usufruct rights over land held by mothers and wives; sharing and cooperation within the sib was common: 'The right to private property exists among the Truk natives. Everyone of them, be it man or woman, adult or child, can own property, for example, land, houses, cattle, money, etc. Whatever the wife brings into the marriage remains wholly at her disposal.'[640] 'The husband and children, however, do have usufruct rights to her property. If she goes back to her family because of death or divorce, then she takes her property with her again. In practice a certain communism prevails, since siblings, members of the same kin group, often live together and cultivate their lands and enjoy the fruits together. This, however, lasts only as long as there are no quarrels among the families.'[641] Elder brothes held supervision rights over property owned by the sib and were expected to care for younger siblings in return: 'In contrast to this indirectly communistic property, there is also that which is directly communistic, for example, land. They call it fanu uon ar puipui, land of their brotherhood. Yet the oldest brother plays a special part in connection with all the property. He not only has the supervision and usufruct right to the property of the younger siblings, which goes back to the common [Page 132] father, but he can also withdraw it from them. Since this not infrequently happens, the oldest is usually also the richest in each family. /121/ The oldest has no rights over the property which the younger ones have obtained from other sources. To make up for the fact that the oldest has such extensive rights, he has the obligation to care for his siblings in certain ways.'[642] The elderly were taken care of within the family: 'In Truk it makes expecially good economic sense to produce at least one child. Parents want children in order to have someone to care for them in old age and to have someone to help with their work. The love of children and of relatives is expressed as based on mutual dependence, and feelings of independence are repressed.'[643]
♠ bravery ♣ present ♥ According to Caughey, the value placed on bravery and competitiveness varied considerably from island to island in the colonial period: 'Ideal character is not defined this way on Fáánakkar. Respectfulness is a major value there but, as we have seen, it is balanced by an equally heavy emphasis on the aggressive and competitive ideals of bravery and strong thought. None of the accounts of modern Romónum, including the life histories, give any indication that these two character values are major preoccupations on that island. It also appears that acts which would be seen as manifestations of bravery on Fáánakkar are [Page 173] viewed as arrogant on Romónum, while actions which could be defined as cowardice on Fáánakkar are viewed as respectful on Romónum. Swartz shows that it is not only inappropriate or unjustified aggression that is considered wrong on Romónum, all aggression is defined as “haughty” (1958:475). Fighting with kinsmen is very bad, fighting with outsiders is less bad, but fighting is haughtiness (1965:32) and “all ‘haughty’ behavior is bad” ( Ibid.:32). One exception is wife beating, “when a woman violates the proscriptions on ‘haughty behavior’ or is disobedient, her husband may beat her” (1958:472) but “these values (of humble or nonhaughty behavior) also apply to men and combine with the threat of divorce to limit overt aggression against his wife” ( Ibid.:473). In all other relationships, even when someone has grossly violated the norms in a haughty way, aggressive retaliation is not approved; “even the punishing of a criminal is seen as aggressive or haughty” (1965:33). In short, on Romónum, “the cultural norm categorically proscribes aggression” ( Ibid.:38). Clearly this is not true on Fáánakkar where people want to be, and want to be known as, persons who are capable of successful aggression. On Fáánakkar the ideal type, the person of “true bravery”, does not usually engage in unjustified or unprovoked aggression (although he shows he is capable of it) but he must be ready and eager to respond competitively and aggressively when challenged, especially by another’s arrogance. To remain humble in the face of another’s challenge is not respectfulness; it is cowardice, and on Fáánakkar, this is despised at least as much as is arrogance.'[644] But he also claims that prior to colonization, belligerence was generally valued in men: 'Any aggression by another is a test of bravery.'[645] 'However, on Romónum it appears that neither the exclusive stress on respectfulness, nor the “prohibition of aggression” (Swartz 1965:37), nor the unaggressive style of social conduct were characteristic prior to the German pacification of Truk in 1903. In the 19th century, the people of Truk as a whole were considered highly aggressive in comparison to those of other areas in Micronesia. “As is well known, 19th century whalers and other travelers avoided Truk because of its reputation for belligerency toward outsiders, and within the atoll itself warfare was frequent. Although the Spanish held title to Truk from 1885 to 1889 and theoretically administered it [Page 174] from their base at Ponape, they actually did not govern the island. The bellicose nature of the people of Truk was a factor in the Spanish avoidance of the area” (Swartz 1965:17). My information about warfare on 19th century Fáánakkar clearly fits this picture as do the reports of Germans who lived on Truk in the early 20th century. “Truk was one big battlefield. Island fought against island, tribe against tribe. On one day two villages were co-operating; on the next day they were fighting each other. There was constant killing in an ever changing situation” (Bollig 1927:104). Evidently aggressiveness was also common on 19th century Romónum. In tracing the history of Romónum from 1850 to 1903, Goodenough notes that the history begins with a fight, and his account shows that it was permeated by assassinations, bloody feuds, sorcery battles, and warfare with neighboring islands (1951:148-161). Gladwin and Sarason (1953:41) provide additional supporting evidence. It also appears that this aggressive behavior was associated with an ideal of bravery similar to that on Fáánakkar. An example from Goodenough’s history of Romónum will illustrate this point. The incident begins just after assassins, who sneaked up to his meeting house and poked their rifles through the thatch wall, have shot and killed the chief Mwaatejinyk: “All of the occupants of the wuut fled in panic except for Wenejóg... Mwaatejinyk’s grandson. He picked up his grandfather’s body and carried it out of the wuut to prevent its falling into the assassin’s hands. Carrying it down to the north shore of the island, he proceeded west along the rocks on the shore and then climbed back up to... where Mwaatejinyk had lived.... Here Wenejóg buried his grandfather in an upright position leaving his head above ground and facing in the direction of the Pwereka 1 house (his enemy’s lineage center).... He then propped Mwaatejinyk’s mouth open with a stick so that his ghost would go and devour the Mwej people in revenge. This deed earned for Wenejóg a great reputation for bravery” (Goodenough 1951:155-156).'[646] 'On Romónum, Fáánakkar, and most of the other Truk islands overt aggression was characteristic throughout the 19th century and an aggressive style of behavior has persisted, in [Page 175] modified form, on Fáánakkar and at least some of the other Truk islands. On Fáánakkar, the traditional values of bravery and strong thought have also persisted, and along with respectfulness, they are still important measures of character. On Romónum overt aggression has very much declined, and the aggressive and competitive components of bravery and strong thought are no longer prominent. It also appears that a shift in the meaning of these terms may have taken place such that several classes of behavior encompassed within the traditional ideal of bravery are now condemned as arrogance. In fact, Swartz believes that the people of modern Romónum “consider all the warfare that went on before foreign control to have been ‘haughty”’ (1965:32).'[647]
♠ respect for property ♣ present ♥ The material is somewhat contradictory. Bollig claims that deliberate destruction of property was a common act of revenge: 'The Truk people are a little nation that always knows how to twist things so that one’s own self does not come out short. Just because everybody thinks only of himself and wants to have everything, damage in material connections is the most sensitive for them. Whoever wants to take revenge on another and deal him a blow need only do some kind of damage against him and he is stricken. That is why damage to houses and canoes, felling trees, uprooting young plants, and killing domestic animals out of a thirst for revenge so frequently occur.'[648] This seems to fit in with Caughey's claim that destruction and theft of property could serve as a demonstration of dominance and bravery: 'To steal openly from others is admirable in that it shows a person’s dominance and demonstrates that he is not intimidated by the aggressive powers of others.'[649] On the other hand, Goodenough claims that property rights were generally respected in social settings and that the threat of social censure served as deterrence against infringements on property rights: 'The possibility of stealing seems to arouse considerable anxiety in the Trukese today as well as formerly, and this despite the fact that they scrupulously respect each other’s rights when in anyone else’s company. They seem always ready to attribute the worst of motives to their fellows.'[650] 'One might assume that dependence on private sanctions for breaches of this kind would provide a situation in which powerful persons and lineages could take advantage of weaker ones. While this has happened, the entire community is likely to unite against a too frequent offender. The property system does not depend, however, on the threat of direct retribution alone. Trouble makers tend to be socially isolated. Even their own lineage mates may eventually withdraw support if their activities too frequently put the lineage in embarrassing situations. These less formal sanctions are usually effective, and a person or group is not likely to violate another’s property rights deliberately and openly except with the definite purpose of precipitating a show-down.'[651] According to Goodenough, chiefs generally avoided direct interference and instead resorted to moral exhortations: 'In aboriginal times no formal action was taken by the community against a thief. It was up to the individual and his corporation (lineage) to collect damages or kill the offender, the latter action frequently leading to a feud with the offender’s lineage. If the identity of a thief was unknown, divination was resorted to in order to discover it. The injured party demanded restitution or payment. If the thief refused, a fight resulted. District chiefs did not interfere directly in such matters. Informants indicated that for them to do so would have been to court the enmity of the entire community. On the other hand, a chief used to preach to his people about the evils of stealing and exhort them to respect each other’s rights in order to hold down dissension within his district.'[652] We have provisionally coded 'present', but expert feedback might be needed on the matter.

♠ Moralistic punishment by human religious agents ♣ absent ♥ absent/present/unknown. The literature makes it clear that the Chuukese believed that transgressions could be punished by supernatural beings[653]. It's worth noting that incest was punished[654]--though it's not clear by whom. eHRAF provides a general description of ritual specialists: 'Ritual practices were conducted by their own specialists. They included spirit mediums, breadfruit summoners, fish summoners, healers, masters of spells, masters of sorcery, builders, navigators, diviners, and most importantly the masters of magic and ritual relating to war and politics. Their knowledge was private property passed down to their children and junior lineage mates.'[655] Spirit mediums were important, but we have found no evidence of them being involved in moralistic punishment: 'In the space of this work we shall consider only the direct residing, which manifests itself in the belief in the nanönu / uanönu/. It is to be recalled that, according to the views of the islanders, the spirit which has appeared at the invocation of the spirits takes up residence on the far. The uanönu is in the service of this far and the spirit sojourning on it. He makes the far, hangs it up, tends to the asor, calls the spirit to jump down from it and settle on his neck. In this condition he speaks with the spirit, asks him questions in the spirit language, presents to him the people’s wishes, and also receives many a revelation. The common people prefer to have recourse to the uanönu in their religious needs. All the spirits, except for Onulap, seem ready to jump down, but especially those of the most recently deceased. The uanönu like to see the souls of deceased children coming down to them, because they have the advantage of being wenewen, that is, sincere, while adults are even able to deceive the uanönu. Some uanönu asserted that never yet had the soul of a deceased Christian come down to them. Anyone /61/ men, women, boys, girls, can be chosen as uanönu or uomar, that is, canoe of the spirit. The choice is wholly at the discretion of the spirit.'[656] 'As soon as someone in the tribe has died, the people gather and tempt the soul so that it will settle down on the far and choose one of the persons present as uanönu. If the spirit grants this plea, the one chosen is regarded as uanönu until the spirit leaves him again. If the people have a request about which the spirit is to give information, he is summoned. The uanönu is the medium. The summoning takes place amid certain ceremonies. At night the people gather in the udd in which the far is hung. A quantity of food and other objects is collected, in part as asor [Page 68] for the önu, in part as gifts for the uanönu, in order to make them both obliging. Then the dancers grasp the kurukur and dance the spirit dance ( dugia), in order to show the spirit their veneration. The uanönu lies calmly on his mat and waits for the right moment. He suddenly feels as though goose flesh were spreading over him. He jumps up, takes hold of the kurukur, and jumps about, while he shouts hi, hi... It is not long before he feels by the heaviness of his neck that the spirit is sitting on him. He stands still, begins to tremble, bends under the weight of his neck, and sometimes even begins to foam. He presses his lips together hard so as not to speak quickly, since he might easily falsely announce the spirit’s words because of hurrying too fast. Finally the people call to him: “ Wäsi me[unknown]inom, tell your revelations.” The uanönu now asks the spirit: “ E seiö, who are you?” (In ordinary speech: en iö?) Whereupon the spirit begins to talk. The people pose their questions, express their wishes, which the uanönu communicates to the spirit in spirit language and receives the answer in the same language. The summoning is not always done in this fussy manner. Some uanönu are so easily susceptible that it suffices to strike them on the arm with an ubud, and they begin to tremble.'[657] 'The performance does not always turn out as desired. It might be that the spirit who settles down is an “ onu[unknown]au or önurot, which the people notice at once by the furious, wild behavior of the uanönu. Then they quickly hide everything dangerous, such as knives, fire, etc., so that the uanönu cannot cause a disaster for anybody, for it has often happened, as the natives asserted, that such a raging one /62/ threw all kinds of things at the heads of those present and ran after them with bush knives. If, on the other hand, the uanönu speaks calmly and softly, the people are delighted, for the spirit is an önusor. Unfortunately, however, the revelations not infrequently turn out to be wholly a fraud. Then the natives grumble about the false uanönu and önumaken (liar spirit), but they do not so quickly lose faith in the business. Next time it will turn out better.'[658] 'The uanönu also has the privilege of seeing the souls of human beings and the spirits in dreams. Thus an uanönu in Param told me one he had seen at night, while sleeping, a number of spirits in the middle of the island, as they danced together. The soul of a man who was still living was also present. That could become dangerous for the poor fellow, however, because how easily might the fickle soul stay away entirely, and then he would have to die. Therefore, as soon as a native hears that a dreaming uanönu has seen his soul, there is nothing more urgent for him to do than to wrap up a few good things, go to the uanönu, and ask him if he would catch again the one that escaped. That is, the uanönu also knows this art. It is called ama[unknown]un. The uanönu makes a certain safei, pours it into a coconut shell, and looks in. All the souls that have flown away now gather in this safei and look like drops of blood. He fishes out the foreign souls by means of a sou and throws them away. The soul of the petitioner, however, remains. The uanönu gives the safei with the captured soul to the unfortunate one to drink and he goes away beaming with joy. He has his soul back and need not die yet, while the uanönu has the gifts. Envious souls on Truk say about the uanönu that they see the most at precisely those times when their chests are the emptiest.'[659] 'There is still another kind of uanönu, called mo[unknown]epui. These indeed see the human souls that have flown away, but do not understand the art of getting them back. They do not play any role among the Truk people.'[660] 'The uanönu has another task during the performance of the spirit dances. The uanönu stands with the safei in the middle of his rows of dancers. This spot is called poönu, that is, bed of the spirit. No one may crowd close to this place. When the procession goes to the dancing place, the uanönu goes before his tribe, his blow-shell in one hand, giddun, /63/ that is, coconut offering for the spirit, in the other. When he steps into the udd, he throws down the coconut. He stations himself wherever it falls, and his dancers crowd around him. He gives them some of the safei to drink, so that the evil sorcery of the troops of dancers and their leaders will not harm them. Then [Page 70] he rubs his shell with curcuma and puts it down with the opening upwards. While the troop is dancing, the uanönu of the other party does all kinds of sorcery in order to disturb the dance. He likewise takes his shell, rubs it with charcoal, and puts it down with the opening downwards. His companions tie an ubud around their necks as a sign for the dancers that they should hang themselves. It now depends on which of the two parties can practice better sorcery. The defeated party is put to shame during the dance. The dancers’ voices fail.'[661] 'A number of binin surround the uanönu. Besides the above-mentioned ones, which also hold for most of his ro[unknown] colleagues, he is not allowed to eat apot or oranges. The people must be careful not to sneeze in his presence, for the spirits do not like that. If the uanönu wants to get rid of his spirit -- which is not at all rare -- he only need bathe in stinking apot water, and the spirit will no longer come to him.'[662] 'There are a great many of these uanönu on Truk. On the island of Fefan, which numbers scarcely 2,000 people, there are more than 90 of them, some of whom still practice their profession. Although these uanönu do not have the authority of the ida[unknown], they still sincerely contribute their share in supporting heathen customs and practices and keeping the spirit cult alive. Many a movement toward Christianity in tribes still heathen was suppressed by the intrigues of the uanönu. Although, on the one hand, many a former uanönu, men and women, came over to Christianity, on the other hand, it was precisely the uanönu still practicing who were the most obstinate opponents of the faith, even more than the ida[unknown].'[663] Other ritual specialists were associated with warfare and politics: 'During the battle the ida[unknown] is near his men. If he wants to see the fighting ended, he throws himself between the lines of the ones fighting and cries, “ e bue le iou,” whereupon the warriors reply, “ e bue le iou (it shall be finished),” and the fighting stops. Anyone who does not obey this call is “bitten” by the ida[unknown] and is killed in the next battle. If there are people of the ida[unknown] who were killed in the battle, they may not be buried immediately, because the ida[unknown] must first inspect their position. Namely, if one lies with his feet toward the region of the enemy, as though he wanted to give him a kick, then there are no difficulties about the burial. But if it is the opposite, then the ida[unknown] first has to bring the corpse into the proper position. While singing, he pushes and turns the dead until his feet fortunately have the desired direction. Only then may his comrades carry him away. As respected as the ida[unknown] is by his own party, so is he hated by the opposite party, since they not unjustly attribute the war to his intrigues. If he falls into the hands of the enemy, he is tortured alive in front of the enemy soönu.'[664] These specialists employed sorcery to retaliate against their enemies and people who slighted them, but we have found no evidence of third-party moralistic punishment: 'The ida[unknown] are also masters in the sphere of sorcery. Their word is regarded as puitikar (hot), which means destructive. An ordinary bite of the ida[unknown] causes a swollen throat. A curse cast by him on an unfortunate person devours the latter’s soul, that is, his life.'[665] 'The occasion for this curse is the anger of the ida[unknown] because the careless so-and-so is so disrespectful as to make noise in his vicinity. It is no wonder that the people are afraid of a man who has such power in his mouth. Yet it is to be added for the vindication of the honor of the ida[unknown] that he also possesses various safei which end the effects of the bites and curses. Good pay, however, is a preliminary condition.'[666] Sorcery was a common means of retaliation: 'What embitters life most for the Truk natives is the boud (sorcery to harm one). The boud occurs in innumerable forms. Nearly all souro[unknown] know how to make the boud with the help [Page 83] of their önu; the ida[unknown] in particular is a feared souboud. Yet there are also very distinct souboud who, with the help of an önu[unknown]au, are able to bring all kinds of trouble to human beings. How often one can hear from sick people the words: “ Emen a boud [unknown]eniei, somebody has cursed me, bewitched me.” The boud prevents women from giving birth, disturbs children’s growth, makes limbs swell, spoils one’s work, steals the [unknown]unuot, and brings inevitable death. Fear of the boud is a true tyranny for the unhappy, heathen Truk people.'[667] 'A radical boud is the uwuli or umuni aramas (roasting human beings). If the souboud cannot get at a person whom he hates, he will kill him symbolically, which soon has as a consequence his actual death. For this purpose he sends his wife into the lölö to search for “his fish” ( iken ro [unknown]). The fishing women know the meaning. As soon as they have caught the fish, they lament the person condemned to death. They bring the fish to the souboud, who breaks the backbone bends it everywhere, and then roasts it. He buries the roasted fish or secretly puts it under the cursed person’s sleeping mat. The fala[unknown] (cookhouse) in which the act has taken place is abandoned and no longer used in the future, because the curse clings to it.'[668] 'The person concerned, however, the one who was sentenced to death in this fashion, still has a chance to escape his fate if he learns about the boud soon enough. He simply has himself /75/ “roasted” again by a souro[unknown], whereby the first “roasting” is neutralized. The souro[unknown] makes a hole in the ground in the house of the person concerned, puts the counter- safei in it, and covers it with hot stones so that it steams. Over this he spreads coconut fronds together with the sleeping mat of the “patient.” The latter now has nothing else to do but to lie on it for a time. The boud is destroyed by this simple method, and the threat to life removed.'[669] 'Even more common than the uwuli aramas is the uwuli ton solä (roasting of thieves). If somebody has had something stolen, he goes to the souatömen so that the latter will help him take revenge on the thief. The souatömen now does the following: [Page 84] He takes a fishing pole and line, ties a small crab to it, sits down in the grass, and sings. In this song he now mentions the names of all those who are suspected of the thievery. As soon as he pronounces the name of the culprit, a kinom or kuen (dark, ugly leguan) jumps out of the grass and bites into the crab. The souatömen seizes the animal, breaks all its bones, and roasts it in a coconut shell. The thief now pines away and must die. But again it is in the hands of the souatömen to put an end to this harsh sentence if the client wishes it in a fit of pity. He rubs the “roasted one” with a protective safei, and the spell is lifted.'[670] 'In former times the natives hardly dared walk across the sandy shore, for the footprints impressed in the soft sand could easily bring about their ruin. If a souboud saw the prints of someone hated by him, he lifted them up, put them in a bowl, and cooked them together with a certain safei. If the sorcery was successful, the feet and legs of the person in question would swell.'[671] 'Jealous women likewise sometimes let themselves be carried away and have recourse to the boud. For example, if a mother sees that the child of her neighbor is growing better than her own, she makes the safeien usiwör, and the bewitched child will become a cripple.'[672] 'The soumaram ( maram, moon) is feared as a sorcerer. In the opinion of the Truk people he has a mysterious connection with the moon. When the moon appears in the west ( sikauru, first quarter) the natives do not dare pronounce the moon’s name in the presence of the soumaram. /76/ Instead of “a li[unknown]maram, the moon shines,” they say, “ a war ewe samol, that chief appears,” since the moon is the chief of the night.'[673] 'Some souboud also have a method for harming people by throwing a sorcery- safei into people’s land or house at night. The most suitable nights for this are those between the first quarter and full moon, and, to be sure, toward morning. The souboud wraps his head, throat, hands and feet with ubud and, swaying his body back and forth while singing, he approaches the house. As a precaution, however, he has a few precious things [Page 85] with him as pen manauan (ransom). Namely, at the time of the boud the occupants of the houses are on the lookout in order to catch a possible souboud. He now hopes, in case he should be detected in his dirty work, to savelhimself from punishment by means of gifts. This does not always seem to succeed, however, for on many islands it is told how such souboud would be paid their reward with the bush knife. The souboud also has a method for recognizing whether his sorcery is effective against a given house or not. He pours safei into a bowl, puts some glowing stones into it, and inoffensively passes the house in question. If the steam of the safei goes toward the house, the boud will be successful; if not, he must repeat it.'[674] However, this does not seem to constitute third-party moralistic punishment. Clarification on the difference between retaliation and punishment is needed.

Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):

♠ physical punishment ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though the literature contains many examples of the Chuukese being punished for crimes such as disobedience[675] and incest[676], it seems that the chief was in charge of punishment, and this variable is meant to capture whether or not religious agents (that is, religious specialists or other religiously motivated individuals) had the authority to inflict moralistic punishment on other people, independently of the chief.
♠ exile ♣ inferred absent ♥ Goodenough mentions a case of a man who had to flee his island due to a feud: 'Remaining in the older house were two sisters, Nijeejice, who was married to Cëëppywn (P3a), and Nijeejinen, who was married to Mwaatejinyk (P2a). In the feud between Pwereka 2 and Pwereka 3a following the murder of Jöku, Cëëppwyn and Mwaatejinyk were on opposing sides, and the former was eventually forced to flee Romonum, going to live for a time on Moen. When Mwaatejinyk became island chief he gave his children the plots of Neemwëc (720-470), Neeceemej (420-460), Neejimwetä (390-450), and Neeram (460-490), and built a house for his wife and daughters at Neeceemej, thereby establishing the Söör 2a descent line. Full or residual title to the soil of these plots is now considered the property of this descent line. When Cëëppwyn returned from exile, he gave his children the land plot Neejöör (540-470), where he, too, built a house for his wife and daughters, who founded the present Söör 2b descent line. All three descent lines continued to operate as a single lineage with one wuut—the old one at Fënykytiw was moved to Neetupw (580-350)—and one fanag, forming an over-all corporation holding full or residual title to the old sööpw lands collectively called Pwowunög.'[677] However, this does not seem to constitute exile in our sense of the term. Moreover, this variable is meant to capture whether or not religious agents (that is, religious specialists or other religiously motivated individuals) had the authority to inflict moralistic punishment on other people, independently of the state (or, in this case, the chief), and this anecdote does not seem to correspond to this description.
♠ loss/seizure of property or wealth ♣ inferred absent ♥ The literature contains examples of chiefs confiscating property from subordinates guilty of disobedience[678], as well as examples of adulterers providing material compensation to their spouses, though it is possible that this practice dated to colonial times[679]. However, this variable is meant to capture whether or not religious agents (that is, religious specialists or other religiously motivated individuals) had the authority to inflict moralistic punishment on other people, independently of the state (or, in this case, the chief), and neither of these examples fits with this description.
♠ loss of status ♣ inferred absent ♥ This variable is meant to capture whether or not religious agents (that is, religious specialists or other religiously motivated individuals) had the authority to inflict moralistic punishment on other people, independently of the state (or, in this case, the chief). Based on the literature, it seems that punishment was usually in the hands of the chief. Moreover, even punishments inflicted by chief (such as disobedience[680]) did not typically entail loss of status.

Extent of punishment (can be more than one):

♠ punishment of rulers ♣ absent ♥ Chiefs and members of chiefly lineages were not liable to punishment: 'Another weak point of the chiefs is partiality. Members of the chief’s kin group, blood relatives, and friends are not readily punished, not even for serious crimes. On the other hand, poor devils whom the chief cannot bear are sentenced to hard labor for the slightest reason or are reported to the government as “disobedient ones” or “rebels.” In the old days the Truk chiefs did away with troublesome persons by means of the knife; today they do it by means of lies and false denunciations.'[681] In a similar vein, elder siblings were not disciplined: 'The eldest son and eldest daughter were treated differently from other children. Their persons were inviolate, their wishes were honored, and they were not liable to physical punishment.'[682]
♠ punishment of elites ♣ absent ♥ Chiefs and members of chiefly lineages were not liable to punishment: 'Another weak point of the chiefs is partiality. Members of the chief’s kin group, blood relatives, and friends are not readily punished, not even for serious crimes. On the other hand, poor devils whom the chief cannot bear are sentenced to hard labor for the slightest reason or are reported to the government as “disobedient ones” or “rebels.” In the old days the Truk chiefs did away with troublesome persons by means of the knife; today they do it by means of lies and false denunciations.'[683] In a similar vein, elder siblings were not disciplined: 'The eldest son and eldest daughter were treated differently from other children. Their persons were inviolate, their wishes were honored, and they were not liable to physical punishment.'[684]
♠ punishment of commoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ This variable is meant to capture whether or not religious agents (that is, religious specialists or other religiously motivated individuals) had the authority to inflict moralistic punishment on other people, independently of the state (or, in this case, the chief). Based on the literature, it seems that punishment was usually in the hands of the chief[685][686].

Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ absent ♥ Note: In the period immediately preceding colonization, the Chuuk Islands were not governed by a state. Instead, the Chuukese/Trukese were divided into multiple clans and lineages sharing resources: 'Chuuk's population is divided into a number of dispersed, matrilineal clans. Within any one district the several lineages are usually but not always of different clans. There are also personal kindreds. As a principle of clan and lineage membership, descent is matrilineal, but otherwise kinship is reckoned bilaterally.' [687] 'The domestic unit was an extended family, based on the women of a lineage or sublineage. It consisted of at least one experienced older woman and two more younger women of childbearing age together with their husbands. Unmarried sons and brothers slept apart in their lineage's meeting house. Extended family households continued through the periods of foreign administration.' [688] District chiefs were chosen from the dominant lineage of an area: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [689] 'A district chiefship was divided between the oldest man in the senior female line in the chiefly lineage and the oldest man in the lineage generally. The latter was executive chief, or "chief of talk," and the former symbolic chief, or "chief of food." Food presentations were made to the symbolic chief. Sometimes the symbolic and executive functions fell to the same individual; often they did not. The symbolic chief was surrounded by his lineage brothers and by his sons, who acted as his agents. These followers and his sisters and daughters were of chiefly rank, distinct from commoners. Through conquest, a lineage might gain the chiefship in more than one district and establish a junior branch as the chiefly lineage in the conquered district. The now subordinate district rendered food presentations to the superordinate one. Most districts were linked in two rival leagues based on competing schools of magic and ritual relating to war, politics, and rhetoric. A chief's authority derived from two things. His lineage's ownership of the district's space entitled him to presentations of first fruits at stated times of the year. More importantly, it gave him authority over the conservation and use of the district's food resources. His authority also derived from his connection with the sky world, its gods, and their superhuman power to accomplish purposes. There was, therefore, a degree of sacredness associated with chiefs.' [690] Traditionally, authority did not extend beyond the atoll and was shared among multiple chiefs on one island. Chuuk was somewhat more fragmented than other Micronesian societies: 'Throughout most of Micronesia the maximum independent autonomous political unit was the high island or the atoll, often subdivided into more than one polity. At the time of European contact, Satawan Atoll in the Mortlocks had four separate communities, each with its own leader, which sometimes fought one another. Palau had two confederations of villages or districts, each independent of the other, and the villages themselves had considerable autonomy. Pohnpei had five petty states, although traditions of a unified rule for the whole island are apparent from an earlier period. Chuuk was extremely fragmented politically, with several independent communities on each of the six larger high islands. The Marshalls and the Gilberts had larger polities and integrated groups of separate atolls under a high chief; these expansionist states achieved their fullest development after the introduction of firearms by Europeans.' [691] Accordingly, many of the below variables are not relevant to the pre-colonial Chuuk.
♠ Price controls ♣ absent ♥
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ ♥
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ absent ♥
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ absent ♥
♠ Pensions ♣ absent ♥
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ absent ♥ Prior to colonization, islanders employed no formal system of weights and measures. Bollig claims that in house construction, materials were measured by eye rather than abstract standards: 'The method of construction of the udd is the simplest imaginable. Four mighty corner posts ( ur) are driven into the ground, on which longitudinal beams ( dilöu) extending across are set and lashed. On the longitudinal beams rest the crossbeams (outam), and, to be sure, in the case of larger udd, three of them, the thick one in the middle. Into the crossbeams are inserted small posts, on which then directly rests the heavy ridge-beam ( uin), which, like all beams, consists of one piece. The beams are not nailed since the old Truk people were unfamiliar with iron. They took wooden pegs or tied the beams together with coconut cord, which can hold for decades. The modern Truk people do use nails. Finally, the rafters are put up and lashed, and the framework is completed. One might think that this work would be done quickly, but far from it, for the natives have to be always changing and turning so that the house will not be crooked and humpy. They are not familiar with measuring instruments; everything is done by eye. Although some udd are beautifully and precisely constructed, nevertheless nothing of true art is to be noted, for it would have to be the rafters, which are crudely notched here and there and even more crudely painted with black, white, or red paint.' [692] Volume was measured in 'handfuls': 'These linear measures are also used in part as dry measures insofar as the people put the finger or the hand into the bowl and thus determine the height from the ground. The hand too is a dry measure; epö is a handful.' [693] So far, no information on weights has been found. However, Bollig mentions some linear measures: 'These linear measures are also used in part as dry measures insofar as the people put the finger or the hand into the bowl and thus determine the height from the ground. The hand too is a dry measure; epö is a handful.' [694] 'They are not all alike in style either. Every island and also every important canoe-builder ( soufalafal) have their own style ( sän). The word sän means rope, because in hewing the keel, /129/ which has considerable influence on the usefulness of the canoe, the measuring is done with a piece of rope. The cances are differentiated according to the method of measuring. Thus, one distinguishes sänin Ku (old name for Mortlock), sänin Poluot, säniged, etc.' [695] Accordingly, we have presumed no standardization of weights and meisures prior to the colonial period.
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ absent ♥ Prior to colonization, Chuukese exchange systems were not monetized. Islanders traditionally engaged in trading expeditions and barter trade: 'At the present time, sailing canoes suitable for long trips remain in use only in the small islands of the Caroline group scattered widely over the ocean from Truk westward. The language and culture found on these islands relate them clearly to Truk, whereas most of them fell historically under the hegemony of Yap to the west—a tie which Lessa (1956) has demonstrated is by no means completely extinct. Both Truk and Yap possess volcanic soils which can support crops impossible of cultivation on the sandy coral islets which lie between. Trade therefore provided the impetus for travel to and from the high islands, and there was in addition considerable social visiting back and forth between all the islands. Some voyages, apparently of exploration, have also been recorded extending over hundreds of miles to Guam and even remoter islands. Trips of up to 200 miles or so are still made, although with reduced frequency. Canoes are also used on a few islands other than those mentioned above for interisland, but essentially local, travel to Truk and Ponape.' [696] 'The acquisition of property on Truk is usually done by means of barter. Lands, houses, and canoes are bartered. Smaller units of exchange are sebi (wooden bowls), deig (curcuma), faupar (red shell disks), hip mats, modesty bands, fishing nets, aromatic oils, coconut cord, home-grown tobacco, and sleeping mats. These products of native industry are not present in like quantity or in like quality on all the islands. Thus, Sapesis on Fefan is famous for its fine bowls, Pol for curcuma and oils, Iluk for its fine hip mats.' [697] 'Weaving generally takes place at certain times, especially when the canoes from the low islands are expected, the inhabitants of which barter for things. In former times not very nice [Page 186] customs were associated with weaving. When the women had completed their work, they put on the new mats and gathered on the shore.' [698] Money was introduced by the colonial administration: 'The impact of these economic changes upon the Trukese was great. Just as under the Germans law and order were made the province of the administration, a step which can be reversed (without chaos) only by a long and intelligently directed course of evolution, so under the Japanese the step to a money economy and dependence upon some categories of imported goods was carried far enough beyond the German beginnings so that it too has become irreversible. While the Trukese were not indoctrinated in the more skilled techniques, such as deep-sea fishing and boat-building, there were many jobs available at manual labor, an ever-increasing flow of trade goods upon which to spend the earnings thereof, and head taxes to assure that those who did not work cut copra. The Trukese began to travel more and more on Japanese boats (many of which they have now taken over and operate), to use a wider variety of Japanese tools, to eat (although not depend upon) rice and canned fish, and to wear clothese exclusively of foreign material.' [699]
♠ Public markets ♣ absent ♥ See above (structures and built environment). Commercial trade was introduced in the colonial period (see FmTrukL for details).

Contracts

♠ State enforcement ♣ absent ♥ So far, we have found no material on contracts. This remains in need of confirmation. See also below.
♠ Non-state enforcement ♣ present ♥ So far, we have found no material on contracts per se. But prior to colonization, senior lineages and dominant clans could grant residential and usufruct rights to junior groups on a provisional basis: 'In each district the lineage with title to its space held the chiefship. The several lineages with full or residual title to plots of soil had full residential rights. Lineages with only provisional titles to plots of soil in grant from other lineages had only conditional residential rights. Lineages with full residential rights maintained symbolic hearths where, with their client lineages, they prepared food to present to the chief in recognition of his lineage's ownership of the space.' [700] Individuals could also grant provisional land rights to other individuals: 'Land was held privately both by individuals and matrilineal, corporate descent groups. Rights in undeveloped space, productive soil, trees and gardens were separable. When soil and breadfruit trees were given in grant, the grantor retained residual rights and the grantee acquired provisional rights. Grantors and grantees could be either individuals or corporations. Full rights went to the survivor on the death or extinction of the other.' [701] We are unsure whether this constitutes a contract according to our Code Book, but have assumed so for the time being. There was no state structure that could enforce provisional titles. However, chiefs occasionally confiscated property granted to junior lineages and provisional title holders in case of disobedience (such as failure to deliver food prestations on the occasion of feasts): 'As noted above, the relations between a chief and the people of his district are patterned largely after the relations between the parties in divided ownership. As provisional title holders to territory, the various lineages owe the chief periodic gifts of produce from the land. These gifts are rendered at stated times in connection with feasts given in the chief’s honor. He fixes the time for these feasts in accordance with a seasonal calendar: two feasts during the breadfruit season, and two more during the turmeric season. The men of the various lineages prepare breadfruit in giant wooden bowls while the women fish. Other types of food are also collected. On the day of the feast each lineage brings its food in a procession, the men carrying the giant bowls on frames and chanting as they walk. The food is carried into the meeting house of the chief’s lineage. The chief then selects the largest bowl for himself and his lineage mates and redistributes the rest of the food to the assembled population. The distribution is made according to lineage and is roughly proportional to the size of each lineage. The contributions which each lineage makes are also proportional to the size of its membership and the amount of productive land which it controls. If a lineage fails in this obligation, the chief has the right to confiscate its holdings. Should he indicate dissatisfaction with the contribution of a particular lineage, its members will hasten to prepare additional food and other gifts to mollify him.' [702] We have presumed this to be a type of 'non-state enforcement' of contracts.
♠ Corporate Agency ♣ absent ♥ So far, we have found no material on contracts. This remains in need of confirmation.
♠ State contracting ♣ absent ♥ So far, we have found no material on contracts. This remains in need of confirmation.
♠ Limited Liability ♣ absent ♥ So far, we have found no material on contracts. This remains in need of confirmation.

♠ between-class formal patronage ♣ absent ♥ So far, we have found no material on patronage relations. This remains in need of confirmation.
♠ within-class informal patronage ♣ absent ♥ So far, we have found no material on patronage relations. This remains in need of confirmation.
♠ between-class informal patronage ♣ absent ♥ So far, we have found no material on patronage relations. This remains in need of confirmation.

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ inferred absent ♥ Note: Prior to colonization, the Chuukese were organized in lineages headed by clans rather than in a state. Accordingly, some of the below variables are irrelevant as they presume a public health framework. Bollig claims that no communal provisions were made for the case of famine: 'Although the natives have sufficient food supplies in normal times, or could have, nevertheless times of famine are not unfamiliar to them. Causes of famine might be a typhoon or a long drought. A lack of rain for three to four months can be disastrous for the islands, since, with the thin layer of humus, all the plantings /166/ die. In such times of famine many cases of death always occur; the families broke up, because everybody was only concerned with saving his own skin. The people cut down the coconut trees and ate the heart (faunepon), which resulted in the death of many. There was no longer any order. Looting theft, and murder were the order of the day. Especially clever ones carried soil onto very high trees and raised some tobacco there.' [703] 'An old saying gives the following advice for a famine: “ nugu sebi, nugu neif, nugu lul. Protect the bowl, the knife, the coconut cord.” That is, one who has a bowl can make himself mogemog (arrowroot) when other foodstuffs are exhausted. This mogemog grows wild in great quantities and, although the Truk people like to eat it, is hardly noticed because it requires too much work. But anyone who also has the other two things besides the bowl, he can climb the coconut palms, cut the blossom stalk, fasten a bowl to it, and thus procure for himself the fortifying palm wine. This gives him a chance to struggle through until better times come.' [704] Islanders relied on crabs and bitter yams in times of famine: 'When fishing is unsuccessful due to unfavorable weather, famine prevails in the huts of the Kanakas, and many a person is hungry and mournfully hangs his head. Others,on the other hand, who are more courageous, roam through the mangrove swamps for mussels or look for crabs in the crab holes on the shore. As bait they hold a fern frond in the crab hole. When the crab seizes the fern with its claws, they quickly pull it out. The natives know very well how to grasp the animal in such a manner that they cannot be pinched by the claws.' [705] 'Yam The yam (Dioscorea sp.), termed eep, is rarely used as a food. According to informant Kekkin on Romonum, this plant was formerly cultivated. The bitter yam, Dioscorea bulbifera, grows wild and is used only as a famine food.' [706] LeBar mentions other 'famine foods': 'Marginal, or famine, foods including: heart of palm (eeryk), Dioscorea bulbifera (pwereka), Ipomoea gracilis (ruke), Ficus tinctoria (jöwöön), Ipomoea digitata (jeteniten).' [707] According to Mahony, Cyrtosperma was stored in swamps for use in times of famine: 'A large number of varieties of Colocasia and Cyrtosperma are recognized but there are only a few varieties of Alocasia, one of which is never eaten. Varieties are classified on the basis of their color, whether or not they have thorns and if so whether many or few, how fast they mature, how large the corm grows and what its consistency and taste are like after being cooked. Almost all of the Colocasia varieties mature in about six months or so. Many of the older varieties of Cyrtosperma take at least three years to mature. Some varieties, such as simiten, are reported to mature in little more than a year. Many of these early maturing varieties are recent introductions but they are very popular, have become quite widespread and are frequently planted. One early maturing variety whose name, six months ( sikismont) is an English translation of the Ponapean name ( sowunpwong wennew), was introduced to Lukunor years ago by a couple of men who worked on a sailing vessel which stopped at Ponape and heighboring islands. It is apparently grown only on Lukunor and a few neighboring islands. However, the magic [Page 95] of its name has gained for it a wide reputation and many people would like to try it out. This variety would be extensively propagated if it were made available, though some might be disappointed since it does not quite live up to its name, taking a year, sometimes a little less than a year, to grow to maturity. In regard to Cyrtosperma, the tasty, rapidly maturing varieties are the most highly desired but they seem to be less hardy. In former times, people used to keep a reserve supply of the hardier but less tasty varieties of Cyrtosperma in the swamps for use in times of famine. This is no longer being done so the hardier varieties are not so frequently propagated.' [708] It is unclear from Mahony's description whether these supplies had a communal character or were only used by individual families. We have provisionally assumed the latter. Expert confirmation is needed on the matter.

♠ provision of famine relief ♣ ♥

♠ Food storage ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to SCCS variable 20 'Food Storage' 'Individual households', not 'Communal facilities', 'Political agent controlled repositories', or 'Economic agent controlled repositories' were present, coded in the SCCS as ‘2’. Bollig and Goodenough describe the fermentation of breadfruit: 'Truk’s small land area and limited animal resources force its inhabitants to rely for food almost exclusively on vegetable and marine resources. The staple food is supplied by the breadfruit tree, which is well suited to the steep slopes, its roots preventing soil erosion. Since it provides a seasonal crop, breadfruit is stored and fermented in pits for the lean period, when the diet is further supplemented with taro, sweet potatoes, and manioc.' [709] 'Since the breadfruit tree does not bear throughout the year, the natives must preserve the surplus so that it, together with the yields from the plantings, will help them through to the next breadfruit harvest. In this period of preserving, all the men and boys are busy with picking ( amomo). With baskets and the iass (picker) they set out in order to relieve the trees of their burden. All the fruit is heaped up in piles at a certain place. A funnel-shaped pit ( nas) has been dug out there and lined with banana leaves. The shell of the fruit is scraped out with a tiger shell ( [unknown]i[unknown]i), which is ground like a plane, the core is cut out, the fruit split into pieces and thrown into the pit. The whole is covered with banana leaves and grass and weighted with large stones. Some time later the fruit begins to ferment, and a yellowish-white mass is formed, which smells sourish.' [710] 'All kinds of breadfruit are preserved together on Truk. On other islands they are carefully sorted out, whereby better apot, mar (name of the preserved food) is supposed to be produced. A mar contains hundreds, even thousands, of fruits. If one considers that each family makes several nas for the lean months, it is clear that one tree cannot feed a family. The apot keeps for years. If more is made in the following year, it [Page 161] is mixed with the old, which is thereby improved. It must be hard to digest, however, since some get a stomachache from eating it. Even at a long distance one can smell the nas, and the breath of those who have eaten apot. For the majority of the natives it is a delicacy. In their “cookbook” there are many recipes for its preparation. They mix it with grated coconut or coconut milk, or knead it in sea water is order to kill the smell somewhat ( marepupu). These recipes, however, are used only on better occasions. Usually it is baked as it comes from the pit.' [711] Fish and other sea animals were roasted and stored in baskets: 'The Truk people still must usually have animal food to accompany the vegetable main dish, or else it will not slide down. The accompanying food is usually fish. The preparation of the fish is a matter for the women and girls, who perform their task with skill and intelligence, although the procedure is a little dirty. The larger fish, with scales and entrails, are simply put on the open fire; the smaller ones, several at a time, are first wrapped in a breadfruit leaf. The fact that, in addition, the cook spits into the fire or blows her nose in it does not spoil the appetite of any true Trukese. During the work she must very carefully watch the animals wandering about, for whatever animals the hut shelters prowl around the fire in order to grab something. Depending upon the circumstances, mussels, cray-fish, and other sea animals are added to the fish on the open fire. After the work is finished, everything roasted is put into the food baskets hanging from the roof, out of which it is then taken as needed. After a very short time under the tropical sun the animals begin to smell. But though the smell may be ever so strong, no Truk native will throw away a good bite on account of it. One does not hear anything about illnesses as a consequence of eating such food. They seem to be completely immune.' [712] We have assumed that stored fish and breadfruit served individual families and domestic units rather than villages or even lineages. This remains in need of confirmation. According to Mahony, Cyrtosperma was stored in swamps for use in times of famine: 'A large number of varieties of Colocasia and Cyrtosperma are recognized but there are only a few varieties of Alocasia, one of which is never eaten. Varieties are classified on the basis of their color, whether or not they have thorns and if so whether many or few, how fast they mature, how large the corm grows and what its consistency and taste are like after being cooked. Almost all of the Colocasia varieties mature in about six months or so. Many of the older varieties of Cyrtosperma take at least three years to mature. Some varieties, such as simiten, are reported to mature in little more than a year. Many of these early maturing varieties are recent introductions but they are very popular, have become quite widespread and are frequently planted. One early maturing variety whose name, six months ( sikismont) is an English translation of the Ponapean name ( sowunpwong wennew), was introduced to Lukunor years ago by a couple of men who worked on a sailing vessel which stopped at Ponape and heighboring islands. It is apparently grown only on Lukunor and a few neighboring islands. However, the magic [Page 95] of its name has gained for it a wide reputation and many people would like to try it out. This variety would be extensively propagated if it were made available, though some might be disappointed since it does not quite live up to its name, taking a year, sometimes a little less than a year, to grow to maturity. In regard to Cyrtosperma, the tasty, rapidly maturing varieties are the most highly desired but they seem to be less hardy. In former times, people used to keep a reserve supply of the hardier but less tasty varieties of Cyrtosperma in the swamps for use in times of famine. This is no longer being done so the hardier varieties are not so frequently propagated.' [713] It is unclear from Mahony's description whether these supplies had a communal character or were only used by individual families. We have provisionally assumed the latter. Expert confirmation is needed on the matter.

♠ provision of food storage ♣ ♥

♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ absent ♥ Note: Prior to colonization, the Chuukese were organized in lineages headed by clans rather than in a state.
